Java -> high level , class-based, object – oriented pg language. Originally developed by Sun
Microsystems in 1995, now owned by oracle. Platform independence: Write once, Run anywhere.
Java emphises simplicity, portability and performance. Object oriented nature allows for modular
and resuable code which is essential to build complex application efficiently. Class based structure
aids in managing and organizing code. Class based structure provides blueprint for creating an
object. Few Implementation dependencies which help developer to focus on problem solving rather
than dealing with platform specific issues. Java has been used in various sectors from finance,
healthcare to android applications.
Java is versatile (ability to perform a wide range of different tasks) and platform independent. Java is
not a language but also a comprehensive platform with distinct editions designed for specific
purposes.
Java SE (Standard Edition)- core functionality for desktop applications. Its core edition on which other
editions are built.
Java EE (Enterprise Edition)- Tools for large-scale, web based applications. Extends SE by providing
additional libraries and tools.
Java ME (Micro Edition)- Lightweight platform for mobile and embedded systems. Ensure smallest
can harness power of java.
Java code can run on any devices as long as JVM (Java Virtual Machine) is present. Java is renowned
for its Robustness (ability to handle errors and unexpected conditions without crashing) and security.
Robustness and security are ensured through strong memory management and security features.
Multithreading supports concurrent execution enhancing performance in high-demand applications.
Java serves as the foundation for Android app development crucial for mobile developers. Supports
the development of cloud-based applications and services offering flexibility and scalability. Integral
to big data technologies like Hadoop enabling processing of large data sets across distributed
systems.
Java is a Powerhouse in multiple sectors. Java is a backbone of financial sector. It powers backend
systems in banking for transaction management and fraud detection. In retail, Java is an engine
behind e-commerce platforms and inventory management systems. It help business keep track of
their stock and manage online-sales efficiently. In healthcare, Java is used for patient management
and data analysis to enhance care. In Telecommunication, Java is used for network management and
billing system.
Java Servlets and JSP(Java Server Pages) enable dynamic web content creation. Spring Framework is
key for building robust, scalable web applications. Java supports Restful web services and APIs for
efficient data exchange and integration between different software systems and allowing seamless
communication. Integrate backend seamlessly with frontend technologies like Angular and React.
Java is primary language for Android app development. Andriod SDK offers tools and APIs for building
versatile mobile apps.
JDK is essential for Java Application Development. It includes JRE for running Java applications. JRE
mainly provides an environment for java program to run while compiler converts Java into bytecode.
JDK provides various development tools for creation, debugging and monitoring. Understanding JDK
is essential to set Java environment effectively.
Installing JDK from Oracle➔ Select JDK version that matches your OS
➔ Ensure compatibility by choosing the correct versio n
➔ Follow step-by-step installation instructions which are provided.
Setting up Environment Variables for JAVA.
➔ Set JAVA_HOME environment variable to the JDK installation path. It is telling to system “hey
this is where my Java lives”
➔ Adding the JDKs bin directly to the system’s PATH variable for easy access. It is basically giving
permission to use Java command from any command prompt or terminal. It is a shortcut to
ur fav app from ur desktop.
➔ Verify the setup by running Java -version in the terminal to check the Java version.it is crucial
as to check whether the system recognizes the java installation.
Installation to IntelliJ IDEA
IntelliJ IDEA is a leading Java IDE known for its Intelligent code completion. It streamlines the coding
process by predicting and suggesting the code as we type, enhancing efficiency. It does not only
speeds up the development but also reduces errors making coding experience more smoother.
Supports wide range of plugins and tools for customization of environment as per specific needs.
Facilitates integration with version control, build tools and databases.
Download and install IntelliJ IDEA
Visit JetBrains website to download IntelliJ IDEA
➔ Choose between Community edition which is free and open source and the Ultimate edition
which comes with additional features(Database tools & Web development support) tailored
for enterprise development. For personal project Community edition will suffice.
➔ Follow installation procedures on their website.
Configure IntelliJ IDEA with JDK for java development
➔ Set JDK path in IntelliJ to enable Java application compilation and execution. Crucial as JDK
allows IntelliJ to run and compile java application.
➔ Navigate to ‘Project Structure’ and select ‘SDKs’ to add the JDK path.
➔ Configure project settings such as SDK, language level and preferences. This involves setting
up SDK, language level and preferences as per development needs.
➔ Install additional plugins to enhance IntelliJ functionality. Additional plugins can provide
support for version controls and database tools like Git, integrate database tools and even
add new programming language. Plugins can improve productivity and streamline the
development process.
Overview of IntelliJ IDE Interface
Project Explorer to navigate through files and directories. Code editor leverages syntax highlighting
for better readability. Error detection to identify error in real-time. Integrated IntelliJ IDEA to provide
built-in tolls like terminal and command-line tasks and manage code changes efficiently with the
version control system.
Common issues and trouble shooting installation

for system to execute and recognise the commands.
➔ Incorrect path settings can cause application issues.
Creating new java project in IntelliJ
➔ Open IntelliJ IDEA and select ‘New Project’ from the main menu
➔ Choose ‘java’ as the project type to work with Java files
➔ Select the appropriate JDK version to determine available Java features
➔ Name ur project and choose a directory for saving it
➔ Click ‘Finish’ to create the project and set up the structure
Project Structure in IntelliJ
➔ The ‘src’ folder is the main area for writing and organizing java source files.
➔ Libraries store external JAR files and dependencies, enhancing project functionality
➔ Configuration files manage project settings and build configurations for smooth compilation
and execution
Configuring project settings and SDK
➔ Access project settings via File > Project Structure for configuration.
➔ Set the Project SDK to the installed JDK version for compiling Java code.
➔ Configure the language level to use specific Java features based on the version.
➔ Adjust compiler settings to optimize the project's build process and performance
Navigating the IntelliJ project explorer
➔ Project Explorer offers a hierarchical view for easy navigation.
➔ Navigate seamlessly between files and folders.
➔ Right-click for context-specific actions like creating new files or directories.
➔ Search functionality aids in quickly locating files within large projects.
Creating Java packages and classes
➔ Right-click the 'src' folder to create a new package.
➔ Use a domain-style naming convention for packages, e.g., 'com.example.project'.
➔ Create new Java classes within your packages.
➔ Organize classes logically within packages for a clean project structure.
➔ Package organization aids in maintaining modular and manageable code.
Managing project dependencies
➔ Dependencies are external libraries essential for your project.
➔ Manage dependencies via Project Structure > Libraries in IntelliJ.
➔ Add JAR files directly or use Maven/Gradle for advanced management.
➔ Ensure all dependencies are compatible with your JDK version to avoid issues.
Using IntelliJ Build tools
➔ Build tools like Maven and Gradle automate code compiling and packaging.
➔ Configure these tools in IntelliJ under Project Structure > Modules.
➔ They manage dependencies and automate the build lifecycle.
➔ Automation ensures consistent and reliable builds, enhancing productivity.➔ Focus more on coding, less on managing the build process.

Understanding java Syntax and Structure
➔ Java programs are structured into
classes and methods, forming the core
of the code.
➔ The main method, 'public static void
main(String[] args)', is the starting
point of any Java application. This
method acts as an entry point where
program begins its execution.
➔ Java is case-sensitive, so identifiers like
'Main' and 'main' are different.
➔ Curly braces define the scope of classes
and methods, ensuring organized and
readable code.
➔ // This is a simple Java program
to demonstrate the basic
structure and syntax
➔
➔ // Define a class named HelloWorld
➔ public class HelloWorld {
➔ // The main method is the entry point of any Java application

➔ System.out.println("Hello, World!");
➔ }
➔ }

Compiling java code in IntelliJ
Coding java code in intellij is a straightforward process of ensuring ur code is free of syntax error
before execution.
➔ Open IntelliJ and create a new project to set up your coding environment.
➔ Write your Java code in the editor to bring your ideas to life.
➔ Navigate to the 'Build' menu and select 'Build Project' to compile your code.
➔ Compilation checks for syntax errors, ensuring your code is ready for execution.➔ Errors, if any, will be displayed in the 'Run' window for you to address.
Running java programs in IntelliJ
➔ Compile your Java code without errors before running.
➔ Execute the program using the 'Run' button or menu in IntelliJ.
➔ View the program's output in the 'Run' window.
➔ Use IntelliJ's debugging tools if the program doesn't behave as expected.
➔ Debugging helps identify and fix issues by stepping through the code.
Using the IntelliJ Debugger
Debugging is a detective in the world of programming that identify and solve the issues in your code
➔ Set breakpoints to pause execution at specific points.
➔ Initiate debugging session by clicking 'Debug'.
➔ Step through code line by line to observe execution flow.
➔ Inspect variables to understand their current state.
➔ Evaluate expressions to gain insights into code behaviour
➔ Debugging helps diagnose and resolve program issues.
Common errors in java programs
➔ Syntax errors occur due to missing semicolons or braces, preventing code compilation.
➔ Runtime errors, like null pointer exceptions, happen during execution and can crash
programs.
➔ Logical errors involve incorrect algorithm implementation, leading to unexpected results.
➔ Compilation errors, such as unresolved symbols, occur when the compiler can't find
references.
➔ Understanding and fixing these errors is essential for successful Java programming.
Best practices for writing java code
➔ Follow naming conventions like camelCase for readability.
➔ Use comments to clarify complex logic and provide context.
➔ Keep methods short and focused on a single task for modularity.
➔ Consistently format code with proper indentation and spacing.
➔ Adhering to these practices enhances code quality and professionalism.
Understanding Java classes and methods
➔ Java classes act as blueprints for creating objects.
➔ Classes define properties through fields (variables).
➔ Methods represent behaviors or actions of a class.
➔ Methods are blocks of code performing specific tasks.
➔ Methods can accept parameters for flexible operations.
➔ Methods can return values to provide operation results.
➔ Understanding classes and methods is key to java’s object-oriented programming.
➔ // Java program demonstrating classes and methods
➔
➔ // Main class definition➔ public class Car {
➔ // Fields of the class
➔ private String color;
➔ private String model;
➔ private int speed;
➔
➔ // Constructor to initialize the fields
➔ public Car(String color, String model) {
➔ this.color = color;
➔ this.model = model;
➔ this.speed = 0; // Initial speed is 0
➔ }
➔
➔ // Method to start the car
➔ public void start() {
➔ System.out.println("The car has started."); // Output: The car has
started.
➔ }
➔
➔ // Method to stop the car
➔ public void stop() {
➔ System.out.println("The car has stopped."); // Output: The car has
stopped.
➔ }
➔
➔ // Method to accelerate the car
➔ public void accelerate(int increment) {
➔ speed += increment;
➔ System.out.println("The car accelerates to " + speed + " km/h."); //
Output: The car accelerates to X km/h.
➔ }
➔
➔ // Method to get the car's current speed➔ public int getSpeed() {
➔ return speed;
➔ }
➔
➔ // Main method to execute the program
➔ public static void main(String[] args) {
➔ // Creating a Car object
➔ Car myCar = new Car("Red", "Toyota");
➔
➔ // Using methods of the Car class
➔ myCar.start();
➔ myCar.accelerate(50);
➔ myCar.accelerate(30);
➔ myCar.stop();
➔
➔ // Displaying the final speed of the car
➔ System.out.println("Final speed: " + myCar.getSpeed() + " km/h"); //
Output: Final speed: 80 km/h
➔ }
➔ }
Java compilation process
➔ Java source code is written in files with a .java extension.
➔ The Java compiler, 'javac', compiles source code into bytecode.
➔ Bytecode is stored in .class files, making it platform-independent.
➔ Java's bytecode can run on any system with a Java Virtual Machine (JVM).
➔ This process enables Java's 'write once, run anywhere' capability.
➔ // This is a simple Java program to demonstrate the compilation process
➔ // We will define a class with a main method
➔
➔ // Define a public class named HelloWorld
➔ public class HelloWorld {
➔ // Define the main method which is the entry point of any Java application
➔ public static void main(String[] args) {➔ // Print a message to the console
➔ System.out.println("Hello, World!");
➔ }
➔ }
➔
➔ // To compile this code, you would use the command: javac HelloWorld.java
➔ // This will generate a HelloWorld.class file containing the bytecode
➔
➔ // To run the compiled bytecode, you would use the command: java HelloWorld
➔ // This will execute the bytecode on the Java Virtual Machine (JVM)
Role of Java Compiler (javav)
➔ 'javac' translates Java source code into platform-independent bytecode.
➔ Checks for syntax errors to ensure code adheres to Java specifications.
➔ Generates .class files containing bytecode, essential for execution.
➔ Acts as a gatekeeper, ensuring only correct code is compiled.
Bytecode and JVM
Bytecode is an pivotal element in java’a ability to maintain platform independence.
➔ Bytecode is an intermediate code generated by the Java compiler.
➔ JVM executes bytecode, ensuring platform independence.
➔ JVM interprets or compiles bytecode into machine-specific code.
➔ Java applications can run on any platform with a JVM.
➔ JVM provides a consistent runtime environment across systems.
Java execution process
➔ Java execution begins with the 'main' method, the program's entry point.
➔ JVM loads the class file containing bytecode instructions.
➔ JVM interprets or compiles bytecode into machine code for execution.
➔ Program runs on the host machine, showcasing Java's platform independence.
➔ // This Java program demonstrates the execution process of a Java application
➔ // It includes the main method, which is the entry point of the program
➔
➔ public class JavaExecutionDemo {
➔ // The main method is the entry point of any Java application
➔ public static void main(String[] args) {
➔ // Print a message to indicate the start of the program
➔ System.out.println("Program started. Loading .class file...");➔
➔ // Simulate the loading of the .class file by the JVM
➔ loadClassFile();
➔
➔ // Simulate the interpretation of bytecode into machine code
➔ interpretBytecode();
➔
➔ // Simulate the execution of the program on the host machine
➔ executeProgram();
➔ }
➔
➔ // Method to simulate the loading of the .class file
➔ private static void loadClassFile() {
➔ System.out.println(".class file loaded by JVM.");
➔ }
➔
➔ // Method to simulate the interpretation of bytecode into machine code
➔ private static void interpretBytecode() {
➔ System.out.println("Bytecode interpreted into machine code.");
➔ }
➔
➔ // Method to simulate the execution of the program on the host machine
➔ private static void executeProgram() {
➔ System.out.println("Program is now running on the host machine.");
➔ }
➔ }
Common compilation error and fixes
➔ Syntax errors occur due to missing semicolons or braces, similar to grammatical mistakes.
➔ Type errors arise from using incompatible data types, like fitting a square peg into a round
hole.
➔ Class not found errors result from incorrect classpaths or misspelled file names.
➔ Review compiler error messages to identify and fix code issues.
➔ Understanding and resolving these errors is crucial for efficient Java development.
➔ // This Java program demonstrates common compilation errors
➔➔ // Importing necessary packages
➔ import java.util.*;
➔
➔ // Defining the main class
➔ public class CompilationErrorsDemo {
➔ // Main method
➔ public static void main(String[] args) {
➔ // Example of a syntax error: missing semicolon
➔ // Uncomment the line below to see the error
➔ // System.out.println("Hello, World!")
➔
➔ // Example of a type error: incompatible data types
➔ // Uncomment the line below to see the error
➔ // int number = "This is a string";
➔
➔ // Example of a class not found error
➔ // Uncomment the line below to see the error
➔ // NonExistentClass obj = new NonExistentClass();
➔
➔ // Correct code
➔ System.out.println("This is a correctly compiled Java program.");
➔ }
➔ }
➔
➔ // To see the errors, uncomment the respective lines and compile the program.
Defining variables in java
➔ Variables in Java act as containers for storing data values.
➔ Each variable is linked to a specific data type, defining the kind of data it can store.
➔ Data types include integers, floating-point numbers, characters, etc.
➔ Variables must be declared with a type and name before use.
➔ Declaration helps the Java compiler understand the data type and usage.
➔ // This Java program demonstrates the use of variables and basic syntax
➔ public class VariableDemo {➔ public static void main(String[] args) {
➔ // Declare an integer variable named 'age' and assign it a value
➔ int age = 25;
➔ // Print the value of 'age' to the console
➔ System.out.println("Age: " + age); // Output: Age: 25
➔
➔ // Declare a double variable named 'height' and assign it a value
➔ double height = 5.9;
➔ // Print the value of 'height' to the console
➔ System.out.println("Height: " + height); // Output: Height: 5.9
➔
➔ // Declare a char variable named 'initial' and assign it a value
➔ char initial = 'J';
➔ // Print the value of 'initial' to the console
➔ System.out.println("Initial: " + initial); // Output: Initial: J
➔
➔ // Declare a boolean variable named 'isStudent' and assign it a value
➔ boolean isStudent = true;
➔ // Print the value of 'isStudent' to the console
➔ System.out.println("Is Student: " + isStudent); // Output: Is Student:
true
➔ }
➔ }
Variable naming conventions
➔ Variable names should be meaningful and descriptive for better readability.
➔ Names must start with a letter, $, or; subsequent characters can include letters, digits, $, or
_.
➔ Use camelCase for variable names: start with lowercase, subsequent words start with
uppercase.
➔ CamelCase helps distinguish variable names from other identifiers in the code.
➔ Meaningful names aid in collaboration and understanding of the code.
➔ // This code demonstrates the use of meaningful variable names in Java
➔ // and follows the camelCase naming convention.
➔➔ public class VariableNaming {
➔ public static void main(String[] args) {
➔ // Declaring a variable to hold a user's age
➔ int userAge = 25;
➔ // Declaring a variable to hold a user's first name
➔ String userFirstName = "John";
➔ // Declaring a variable to hold a user's last name
➔ String userLastName = "Doe";
➔
➔ // Printing the user's details
➔ System.out.println("User's Age: " + userAge);
➔ // Execute the code to see the output of the user's age
➔ System.out.println("User's First Name: " + userFirstName);
➔ // Execute the code to see the output of the user's first name
➔ System.out.println("User's Last Name: " + userLastName);
➔ // Execute the code to see the output of the user's last name
➔ }
➔ }
Declaring and Initializing Variables
➔ Declaring a variable involves specifying its type and name.
➔ Initialization assigns a value to the declared variable.
➔ Declaration and initialization can be combined in one line for efficiency.
➔ Example: 'int age = 25;' declares and initializes an integer variable.
➔ Combining these steps ensures the variable is ready for immediate use.
➔ This practice enhances code clarity and reduces potential errors.
➔ // This code demonstrates variable declaration and initialization in Java
➔
➔ public class VariableExample {
➔ public static void main(String[] args) {
➔ // Declaring a variable
➔ int age;
➔ // Initializing the variable
➔ age = 25;➔ // Output the value of age
➔ System.out.println("The age is: " + age); // The age is: 25
➔
➔ // Declaring and initializing a variable in one line
➔ int year = 2023;
➔ // Output the value of year
➔ System.out.println("The year is: " + year); // The year is: 2023
➔
➔ // Another example of declaring and initializing a variable
➔ String name = "John";
➔ // Output the value of name
➔ System.out.println("The name is: " + name); // The name is: John
➔
➔ // Demonstrating the use of variables in a simple calculation
➔ int nextYearAge = age + 1;
➔ // Output the calculated value
➔ System.out.println("Next year, the age will be: " + nextYearAge); //
Next year, the age will be: 26
➔ }
➔ }
Scope and Lifetime of vairables
➔ Variable scope defines where a variable can be accessed in the code.
➔ Local variables are declared inside methods or blocks and are temporary.
➔ Local variables exist only during the method's execution.
➔ Instance variables are declared within a class but outside methods.
➔ Instance variables are accessible to all methods in the class.
➔ Instance variables exist as long as the class object exists.
➔ // This Java program demonstrates the concept of variable scope and lifetime
➔
➔ // Class to demonstrate instance and local variables
➔ public class VariableScopeExample {
➔ // Instance variable
➔ private int instanceVariable = 10;➔
➔ // Method to demonstrate local variable
➔ public void demonstrateLocalVariable() {
➔ // Local variable
➔ int localVariable = 5;
➔
➔ // Accessing local variable
➔ System.out.println("Local Variable: " + localVariable);
➔ // Accessing instance variable
➔ System.out.println("Instance Variable: " + instanceVariable);
➔ }
➔
➔ // Main method to run the program
➔ public static void main(String[] args) {
➔ // Creating an object of VariableScopeExample
➔ VariableScopeExample example = new VariableScopeExample();
➔
➔ // Calling the method to demonstrate local variable
➔ example.demonstrateLocalVariable();
➔
➔ // Trying to access localVariable here would cause an error
➔ // System.out.println(localVariable); // Uncommenting this line will
cause a compilation error
➔ }
➔ }
Common Mistakes in variables
➔ Common mistake: Not initializing variables before use leads to compilation errors.
➔ Avoid using reserved keywords like 'int' or 'class' as variable names.
➔ Mismatching data types during assignment, such as assigning a string to an integer, causes
errors.
➔ Awareness of these pitfalls helps in writing cleaner and more efficient Java code.
➔ // Java code to demonstrate common pitfalls with variables
➔➔ public class VariablePitfalls {
➔ public static void main(String[] args) {
➔ // Pitfall 1: Using a variable without initializing it
➔ // int uninitializedVariable; // Uncommenting this line will cause a
compilation error
➔ int initializedVariable = 10; // Correctly initialized variable
➔ System.out.println("Initialized Variable: " + initializedVariable); //
Output: Initialized Variable: 10
➔
➔ // Pitfall 2: Using reserved keywords as variable names
➔ // int class = 5; // Uncommenting this line will cause a compilation
error
➔ int validVariableName = 5; // Correctly named variable
➔ System.out.println("Valid Variable Name: " + validVariableName); //
Output: Valid Variable Name: 5
➔
➔ // Pitfall 3: Mismatching data types during assignment
➔ // int number = "string"; // Uncommenting this line will cause a
compilation error
➔ int number = 20; // Correctly assigned integer value
➔ System.out.println("Number: " + number); // Output: Number: 20
➔ }
➔ }
Hands-on: Writing Simple Java Programs
➔ Declare and initialize variables to calculate the area of a rectangle.
➔ Reinforce understanding of variable declaration and initialization.
➔ Use variables to store the length and width of the rectangle.
➔ Compute the area by multiplying length and width.
➔ Apply concepts in a practical Java programming scenario.
➔ // Java program to calculate the area of a rectangle
➔
➔ public class RectangleArea {
➔ public static void main(String[] args) {➔ // Declare and initialize variables for length and width
➔ int length = 10; // Length of the rectangle
➔ int width = 5; // Width of the rectangle
➔
➔ // Calculate the area of the rectangle
➔ int area = length * width; // Area formula: length * width
➔
➔ // Print the area of the rectangle
➔ System.out.println("The area of the rectangle is: " + area);
➔ // Execute the code to see the output of the area calculation
➔ }
➔ }
Overview of Java Primitive Data Types
➔ They are the fundamental building blocks for data manipulation in Java.
➔ Java provides 8 primitive data types: byte, short, int, long, float, double, char, and boolean.
➔ Primitive data types are predefined by Java and identified by specific keywords.
➔ Primitive types hold their values directly in memory, unlike objects.
➔ Understanding these types is crucial for writing efficient Java code.
Characteristics of int, float, double, char
➔ 'int': 32-bit signed integer, range from -2^31 to 2^31-1.
➔ 'float': 32-bit floating point, suitable for single-precision calculations.
➔ 'double': 64-bit floating point, provides double-precision for higher accuracy.
➔ 'char': 16-bit Unicode character, range from 0 to 65,535, supports diverse characters.
Understanding Boolean Data Type
➔ Boolean data type can only be true or false.
➔ Useful for tracking true/false conditions, like game levels.
➔ Integral in control flow statements such as if-else.
➔ Enables decision-making in programming logic.
➔ // This Java program demonstrates the use of primitive data types and basic
operations
➔
➔ // Importing necessary packages
➔ import java.util.Scanner;
➔
➔ public class PrimitiveTypesExample {➔ public static void main(String[] args) {
➔ // Declaring and initializing various primitive types
➔ int integerExample = 10;
➔ float floatExample = 20.5f;
➔ double doubleExample = 30.5;
➔ char charExample = 'A';
➔
➔ // Printing the initialized values
➔ System.out.println("Integer value: " + integerExample);
➔ System.out.println("Float value: " + floatExample);
➔ System.out.println("Double value: " + doubleExample);
➔ System.out.println("Char value: " + charExample);
➔
➔ // Performing basic arithmetic operations
➔ int sum = integerExample + 5;
➔ double average = (integerExample + floatExample + doubleExample) / 3;
➔
➔ // Printing the results of arithmetic operations
➔ System.out.println("Sum of integerExample and 5: " + sum);
➔ System.out.println("Average of integerExample, floatExample, and
doubleExample: " + average);
➔
➔ // Using boolean values in control flow
➔ boolean hasSubscription = true;
➔
➔ // Control flow statement using boolean
➔ if (hasSubscription) {
➔ System.out.println("User has access to premium features.");
➔ } else {
➔ System.out.println("User does not have access to premium
features.");
➔ }
➔ }➔ }
Introduction to the string and string class
➔ Strings in Java are objects representing sequences of characters.
➔ The String class is part of the java.lang package, offering various methods for manipulation.
➔ Strings are immutable, meaning they cannot be changed once created.
➔ Immutability enhances performance and security, allowing safe sharing across programs.
➔ Immutable strings can be cached and reused, reducing memory allocation needs.
➔ // Importing necessary package
➔ import java.util.Arrays;
➔
➔ public class StringExample {
➔ public static void main(String[] args) {
➔ // Creating a string using a string literal
➔ String greeting = "Hello, World!";
➔
➔ // Demonstrating immutability
➔ // Attempting to change the string will create a new string object
➔ String newGreeting = greeting.replace("World", "Java");
➔
➔ // Printing the original and new strings
➔ System.out.println("Original String: " + greeting);
➔ // Execute this line to see the output of the original string
➔ System.out.println("New String: " + newGreeting);
➔ // Execute this line to see the output of the new string
➔
➔ // Using some String class methods
➔ // Converting string to uppercase
➔ String upperCaseGreeting = greeting.toUpperCase();
➔ System.out.println("Uppercase String: " + upperCaseGreeting);
➔ // Execute this line to see the output of the uppercase string
➔
➔ // Splitting the string into an array of words
➔ String[] words = greeting.split(", ");
➔ System.out.println("Split String: " + Arrays.toString(words));➔ // Execute this line to see the output of the split string
➔
➔ // Checking the length of the string
➔ int length = greeting.length();
➔ System.out.println("Length of String: " + length);
➔ // Execute this line to see the output of the string length
➔ }
➔ }
Creating and Initializing Strings
➔ Strings can be created using string literals or the 'new' keyword.
➔ String literals are stored in the string pool for memory efficiency.
➔ Using string literals reuses existing strings in the pool if identical.
➔ The 'new' keyword creates a new object in the heap memory.
➔ Understanding string creation impacts performance and memory usage.
➔ // Demonstrating string creation in Java using literals and the 'new' keyword
➔
➔ public class StringCreationExample {
➔ public static void main(String[] args) {
➔ // Creating strings using string literals
➔ // These strings are stored in the string pool
➔ String literalString1 = "Hello";
➔ String literalString2 = "Hello";
➔
➔ // Creating strings using the 'new' keyword
➔ // These strings are stored in the heap memory
➔ String newString1 = new String("Hello");
➔ String newString2 = new String("Hello");
➔
➔ // Comparing string references
➔ // This will print 'true' because both refer to the same object in the
string pool
➔ System.out.println("literalString1 == literalString2: " +
(literalString1 == literalString2)); // true➔
➔ // This will print 'false' because 'new' creates a new object in the
heap
➔ System.out.println("newString1 == newString2: " + (newString1 ==
newString2)); // false
➔
➔ // Comparing string content
➔ // This will print 'true' because the content of the strings is the
same
➔ System.out.println("literalString1.equals(newString1): " +
literalString1.equals(newString1)); // true
➔ }
➔ }
String Immutability and Memory
➔ String immutability enhances thread safety by preventing concurrent modifications.
➔ Immutable strings can be shared across multiple threads without synchronization.
➔ The string pool optimizes memory by reusing references to existing immutable strings.
➔ Java checks the string pool for existing strings to avoid creating duplicates.
Common String Methods and Operations
➔ Java's String class offers essential methods for string manipulation.
➔ length() returns the number of characters in a string.
➔ charAt() retrieves a character at a specified index.
➔ substring() extracts a portion of a string.
➔ toUpperCase() and toLowerCase() change the string's case.
➔ These methods are vital for various string operations in Java.
➔ // Importing necessary packages

➔ }String Comparison and Equality
➔ equals() compares the content of two strings for equality.
➔ '==' checks if two references point to the same memory object.
➔ equalsIgnoreCase() compares strings without considering case differences.
➔ Understanding these methods is crucial for accurate string comparison in Java.
➔ // Importing necessary packages
➔ import java.util.ArrayList;
➔ import java.util.List;
➔
➔ public class StringComparison {
➔ public static void main(String[] args) {
➔ // Creating a list to store string pairs for comparison
➔ List<String[]> stringPairs = new ArrayList<>();
➔
➔ // Adding string pairs to the list
➔ stringPairs.add(new String[]{"Hello", "Hello"});
➔ stringPairs.add(new String[]{"Hello", "hello"});
➔ stringPairs.add(new String[]{"Java", "Java"});
➔ stringPairs.add(new String[]{"Java", "java"});
➔ stringPairs.add(new String[]{"World", "world"});
➔
➔ // Iterating over each pair of strings
➔ for (String[] pair : stringPairs) {
➔ String str1 = pair[0];
➔ String str2 = pair[1];
➔➔ // Comparing strings using equals() method
➔ System.out.println("Comparing '" + str1 + "' and '" + str2 + "'
using equals(): " + str1.equals(str2));
➔ // Execute the above line to see the output of equals() comparison
➔
➔ // Comparing strings using '==' operator
➔ System.out.println("Comparing '" + str1 + "' and '" + str2 + "'
using '==': " + (str1 == str2));
➔ // Execute the above line to see the output of '==' comparison
➔
➔ // Comparing strings using equalsIgnoreCase() method
➔ System.out.println("Comparing '" + str1 + "' and '" + str2 + "'
using equalsIgnoreCase(): " + str1.equalsIgnoreCase(str2));
➔ // Execute the above line to see the output of equalsIgnoreCase()
comparison
➔
➔ // Adding a separator for clarity in output
➔ System.out.println("-----------------------------");
➔ }
➔ }
➔ }

Overview of Java Operators➔ Operators in Java are special symbols used for operations on variables and values.
➔ They are essential for calculations, decision-making, and program flow control.
➔ Java operators are categorized into arithmetic, assignment, relational, and logical types.
➔ This lesson focuses on arithmetic and assignment operators, crucial for calculations and
value assignments.
Using Arithmetic Operators
➔ Arithmetic operators are essential for mathematical operations in Java.
➔ Include operators: + (addition), - (subtraction), (multiplication), / (division), % (modulus).
➔ Operate on numeric data types like int, float, and double.
➔ The '+' operator adds numbers; '%' finds the remainder of a division.
➔ Crucial for performing calculations and logic in Java programming.
➔ // Java program to demonstrate arithmetic operators
➔ public class ArithmeticOperatorsDemo {
➔ public static void main(String[] args) {
➔ // Declare and initialize variables
➔ int num1 = 20;
➔ int num2 = 10;
➔
➔ // Addition
➔ int sum = num1 + num2;
➔ System.out.println("Sum: " + sum); // Output: Sum: 30
➔
➔ // Subtraction
➔ int difference = num1 - num2;
➔ System.out.println("Difference: " + difference); // Output: Difference:
10
➔
➔ // Multiplication
➔ int product = num1 * num2;
➔ System.out.println("Product: " + product); // Output: Product: 200
➔
➔ // Division
➔ int quotient = num1 / num2;
➔ System.out.println("Quotient: " + quotient); // Output: Quotient: 2
➔➔ // Modulus
➔ int remainder = num1 % num2;
➔ System.out.println("Remainder: " + remainder); // Output: Remainder: 0
➔
➔ // Demonstrating with float
➔ float num3 = 15.5f;
➔ float num4 = 4.2f;
➔
➔ // Division with float
➔ float floatQuotient = num3 / num4;
➔ System.out.println("Float Quotient: " + floatQuotient); // Output:
Float Quotient: 3.6904762
➔
➔ // Modulus with float
➔ float floatRemainder = num3 % num4;
➔ System.out.println("Float Remainder: " + floatRemainder); // Output:
Float Remainder: 2.9
➔ }
➔ }
Understanding Assignment Operators
➔ Assignment operators assign values to variables, crucial for programming.
➔ The basic assignment operator '=' assigns the right-side value to the left-side variable.
➔ Compound assignment operators (+=, -=, *, /=, %=) combine arithmetic with assignment.
➔ 'a += b' is shorthand for 'a = a + b', simplifying code and enhancing readability.
➔ Compound operators are useful in loops and iterative processes for updating values.
➔ // Importing necessary packages
➔ import java.util.*;
➔
➔ // Main class
➔ public class AssignmentOperatorsExample {
➔ public static void main(String[] args) {
➔ // Initialize variables
➔ int a = 10;➔ int b = 5;
➔
➔ // Basic assignment operator
➔ // Assigning value of b to a
➔ a = b;
➔ // Print the result
➔ System.out.println("After a = b, a: " + a); // Execute to see the
output
➔
➔ // Compound assignment operators
➔ // Using += operator
➔ a += b; // Equivalent to a = a + b
➔ // Print the result
➔ System.out.println("After a += b, a: " + a); // Execute to see the
output
➔
➔ // Using -= operator
➔ a -= b; // Equivalent to a = a - b
➔ // Print the result
➔ System.out.println("After a -= b, a: " + a); // Execute to see the
output
➔
➔ // Using *= operator
➔ a *= b; // Equivalent to a = a * b
➔ // Print the result
➔ System.out.println("After a *= b, a: " + a); // Execute to see the
output
➔
➔ // Using /= operator
➔ a /= b; // Equivalent to a = a / b
➔ // Print the result
➔ System.out.println("After a /= b, a: " + a); // Execute to see the
output➔
➔ // Using %= operator
➔ a %= b; // Equivalent to a = a % b
➔ // Print the result
➔ System.out.println("After a %= b, a: " + a); // Execute to see the
output
➔ }
➔ }
Operator Precedence and Associativity
➔ Operator precedence dictates the order of operations in expressions.
➔ Higher precedence operators are evaluated before lower precedence ones.
➔ Multiplication and division have higher precedence than addition and subtraction.
➔ Associativity determines the evaluation order for operators with the same precedence.
➔ Most Java operators are left-associative, evaluated from left to right.
➔ Understanding precedence and associativity is crucial for correct and efficient expressions.
➔ // Importing necessary packages
➔ import java.util.*;
➔
➔ public class OperatorPrecedenceExample {
➔ public static void main(String[] args) {
➔ // Demonstrating operator precedence
➔ int result1 = 3 + 4 * 5; // Multiplication has higher precedence than
addition
➔ System.out.println("Result of 3 + 4 * 5: " + result1); // Execute to
see the output
➔
➔ // Demonstrating associativity
➔ int result2 = 100 / 10 * 2; // Left-associative: (100 / 10) * 2
➔ System.out.println("Result of 100 / 10 * 2: " + result2); // Execute to
see the output
➔
➔ // Demonstrating right-associative operator
➔ int a, b, c;➔ a = b = c = 10; // Right-associative: a = (b = (c = 10))
➔ System.out.println("Values after right-associative assignment: a = " +
a + ", b = " + b + ", c = " + c); // Execute to see the output
➔
➔ // Using parentheses to change precedence
➔ int result3 = (3 + 4) * 5; // Parentheses change the order of
operations
➔ System.out.println("Result of (3 + 4) * 5: " + result3); // Execute to
see the output
➔ }
➔ }
Common Pitfalls with Operators
➔ Operator precedence can lead to unexpected results if misunderstood.
➔ Mixing operations without parentheses can lead to surprising outcomes.
➔ Integer division results in an integer, causing potential precision loss.
➔ Compound assignment operators can cause unexpected type conversions.
➔ Be aware of data type interactions with compound assignments.
➔ // Java code to demonstrate common pitfalls with operators
➔
➔ public class OperatorPitfalls {
➔ public static void main(String[] args) {
➔ // Demonstrating operator precedence
➔ int result1 = 5 + 3 * 2; // Without parentheses
➔ int result2 = (5 + 3) * 2; // With parentheses
➔ System.out.println("Result without parentheses: " + result1); //
Output: 11
➔ System.out.println("Result with parentheses: " + result2); // Output:
16
➔
➔ // Demonstrating integer division
➔ int divisionResult = 5 / 2;
➔ System.out.println("Integer division result: " + divisionResult); //
Output: 2➔
➔ // Demonstrating compound assignment with different data types
➔ int integerValue = 5;
➔ double doubleValue = 2.5;
➔ integerValue += doubleValue; // Compound assignment
➔ System.out.println("Result after compound assignment: " +
integerValue); // Output: 7
➔ }
➔ }

➔ Practice string concatenation using the + operator, StringBuilder, and StringBuffer.
// This Java code demonstrates the use of arithmetic and assignment operators
// It also explores operator precedence and common pitfalls like integer division
public class OperatorPractice {
public static void main(String[] args) {
// Arithmetic operations
int a = 10;
int b = 5;
int sum = a + b; // Addition
int difference = a - b; // Subtraction
int product = a * b; // Multiplication
int quotient = a / b; // Division
int remainder = a % b; // Modulus
// Print results of arithmetic operations
System.out.println("Sum: " + sum); // Execute to see the sum
System.out.println("Difference: " + difference); // Execute to see the difference
System.out.println("Product: " + product); // Execute to see the product
System.out.println("Quotient: " + quotient); // Execute to see the quotient
System.out.println("Remainder: " + remainder); // Execute to see the remainder// Operator precedence
int result = 3 + 4 * 5; // Multiplication has higher precedence than addition
System.out.println("Result of 3 + 4 * 5: " + result); // Execute to see the result
// Integer division pitfall
int intDiv = 5 / 2; // Integer division
System.out.println("Integer division of 5 / 2: " + intDiv); // Execute to see the integer division
result
// Type conversion with compound assignment
double c = 5.0;
c += 2; // Compound assignment with type conversion
System.out.println("Result of compound assignment c += 2: " + c); // Execute to see the result
}
}
Methods of String Concatenation
➔ String concatenation combines multiple strings into one.
➔ Several methods available: + operator, String.concat(), StringBuilder, and StringBuffer.
➔ The + operator is simple and intuitive for quick concatenations.
➔ StringBuilder and StringBuffer are efficient for numerous concatenations.
➔ Choosing the right method depends on performance needs and application requirements.
➔ // Importing necessary classes
➔ import java.util.*;
➔
➔ public class StringConcatenationExample {
➔ public static void main(String[] args) {
➔ // Using the plus operator for string concatenation
➔ String firstName = "John";
➔ String lastName = "Doe";
➔ String fullName = firstName + " " + lastName;
➔ // Print the full name using plus operator
➔ System.out.println("Full Name using + operator: " + fullName);➔ // Execute this line to see the output
➔
➔ // Using String.concat() method
➔ String greeting = "Hello";
➔ String completeGreeting = greeting.concat(", ").concat(fullName);
➔ // Print the complete greeting using concat method
➔ System.out.println("Greeting using concat(): " + completeGreeting);
➔ // Execute this line to see the output
➔
➔ // Using StringBuilder for efficient concatenation
➔ StringBuilder sb = new StringBuilder();
➔ sb.append("Welcome ");
➔ sb.append(fullName);
➔ sb.append(" to the Java world!");
➔ // Print the message using StringBuilder
➔ System.out.println("Message using StringBuilder: " + sb.toString());
➔ // Execute this line to see the output
➔
➔ // Using StringBuffer for thread-safe concatenation
➔ StringBuffer sbf = new StringBuffer();
➔ sbf.append("Goodbye ");
➔ sbf.append(fullName);
➔ sbf.append(", see you soon!");
➔ // Print the message using StringBuffer
➔ System.out.println("Message using StringBuffer: " + sbf.toString());
➔ // Execute this line to see the output
➔ }
➔ }
Using the + Operator for Strings
➔ The + operator is the simplest method for string concatenation in Java.
➔ Automatically converts non-string operands to strings, enhancing versatility.
➔ Ideal for small-scale concatenations due to its simplicity.➔ Creates a new String object each time, leading to performance issues in loops or large-scale
operations.
➔ Consider alternative methods for more complex or performance-critical tasks.
➔ // Importing necessary classes
➔ import java.util.ArrayList;
➔
➔ // Main class
➔ public class StringConcatenationExample {
➔ public static void main(String[] args) {
➔ // Using the + operator for simple string concatenation
➔ String greeting = "Hello";
➔ String name = "World";
➔ String message = greeting + ", " + name + "!";
➔ // Print the concatenated message
➔ System.out.println(message); // Execute this line to see the output
➔
➔ // Demonstrating the inefficiency of + operator in loops
➔ ArrayList<String> words = new ArrayList<>();
➔ words.add("Java");
➔ words.add("is");
➔ words.add("fun");
➔
➔ String sentence = "";
➔ for (String word : words) {
➔ sentence += word + " ";
➔ }
➔ // Print the concatenated sentence
➔ System.out.println(sentence.trim()); // Execute this line to see the
output
➔
➔ // Using StringBuilder for efficient concatenation
➔ StringBuilder sb = new StringBuilder();
➔ for (String word : words) {
➔ sb.append(word).append(" ");➔ }
➔ // Print the concatenated sentence using StringBuilder
➔ System.out.println(sb.toString().trim()); // Execute this line to see
the output
➔ }
➔ }
StringBuilder and StringBuffer
➔ StringBuilder and StringBuffer allow for mutable strings, enabling modifications without
creating new objects.
➔ StringBuilder is optimized for single-threaded environments due to its speed.
➔ StringBuffer is thread-safe, making it suitable for multi-threaded applications.
➔ Both classes offer better performance for extensive string modifications compared to the +
operator.
➔ // Importing necessary classes
➔ import java.util.concurrent.ExecutorService;
➔ import java.util.concurrent.Executors;
➔
➔ public class StringManipulation {
➔ public static void main(String[] args) {
➔ // Using StringBuilder for single-threaded environment
➔ // Initialize a StringBuilder object
➔ StringBuilder sb = new StringBuilder("Hello");
➔ // Append strings to the StringBuilder object
➔ sb.append(" World");
➔ sb.append("!");
➔ // Print the final string
➔ System.out.println("StringBuilder result: " + sb.toString());
➔ // Execute this line to see the output of StringBuilder
➔
➔ // Using StringBuffer for multi-threaded environment
➔ // Initialize a StringBuffer object
➔ StringBuffer sbf = new StringBuffer("Hello");
➔ // Create a thread pool with two threads➔ ExecutorService executor = Executors.newFixedThreadPool(2);
➔ // Define a task to append strings to the StringBuffer object
➔ Runnable task = () -> {
➔ sbf.append(" World");
➔ sbf.append("!");
➔ };
➔ // Execute the task with two threads
➔ executor.execute(task);
➔ executor.execute(task);
➔ // Shutdown the executor service
➔ executor.shutdown();
➔ // Print the final string
➔ System.out.println("StringBuffer result: " + sbf.toString());
➔ // Execute this line to see the output of StringBuffer
➔ }
➔ }
Formatting Strings with printf
➔ Java's printf method formats strings like C's printf.
➔ Supports format specifiers for precise output control.
➔ Handles integers, floating-point numbers, and strings.
➔ Ideal for creating well-organized console outputs.
➔ Enhances readability in reports and tables.
➔ // Importing the necessary package for input-output operations
➔ import java.util.Locale;
➔
➔ public class PrintfExample {
➔ public static void main(String[] args) {
➔ // Demonstrating the use of printf to format integers
➔ int intValue = 12345;
➔ System.out.printf("Integer with width 10: %10d\n", intValue);
➔ // Execute this line to see the formatted integer output
➔
➔ // Demonstrating the use of printf to format floating-point numbers➔ double doubleValue = 12345.6789;
➔ System.out.printf("Floating-point with 2 decimal places: %.2f\n",
doubleValue);
➔ // Execute this line to see the formatted floating-point output
➔
➔ // Demonstrating the use of printf to format strings
➔ String stringValue = "Hello, World!";
➔ System.out.printf("String with width 20: %20s\n", stringValue);
➔ // Execute this line to see the formatted string output
➔
➔ // Example of formatting a financial report
➔ String item = "Revenue";
➔ double amount = 1234567.89;
➔ System.out.printf(Locale.US, "%15s: $%,.2f\n", item, amount);
➔ // Execute this line to see the formatted financial report output
➔ }
➔ }
Hands-on: String formatting tasks
➔ Practice string concatenation using the + operator, StringBuilder, and StringBuffer.
// Importing necessary classes
import java.util.*;
public class StringConcatenationExample {

// Execute this line to see the output of the plus operator concatenation// Using StringBuilder for string concatenation
StringBuilder sb = new StringBuilder();
sb.append("Hello");
sb.append(" ");
sb.append("World");
// Print the result of concatenation using StringBuilder
System.out.println("Using StringBuilder: " + sb.toString());
// Execute this line to see the output of the StringBuilder concatenation
// Using StringBuffer for string concatenation
StringBuffer sbf = new StringBuffer();
sbf.append("Hello");
sbf.append(" ");
sbf.append("World");
// Print the result of concatenation using StringBuffer
System.out.println("Using StringBuffer: " + sbf.toString());
// Execute this line to see the output of the StringBuffer concatenation
// Using printf for formatted output
int number = 42;
String text = "apples";
// Print formatted output using printf
System.out.printf("I have %d %s.%n", number, text);
// Execute this line to see the formatted output using printf
}
}
Implicit vs Explicit Type Conversion
➔ Type conversion in Java is essential for converting data types.
➔ Implicit conversion is automatic and prevents data loss.
➔ Occurs when converting smaller data types to larger ones.
➔ Explicit conversion, or casting, is manual and can lead to data loss.➔ Necessary for converting larger data types to smaller ones.
➔ // Java program to demonstrate type conversion and casting
➔
➔ public class TypeConversionExample {
➔ public static void main(String[] args) {
➔ // Implicit conversion example
➔ // Declare an integer variable
➔ int intValue = 100;
➔ // Automatically convert int to long
➔ long longValue = intValue;
➔ // Print the long value
➔ System.out.println("Implicit conversion from int to long: " +
longValue);
➔
➔ // Explicit conversion example
➔ // Declare a double variable
➔ double doubleValue = 9.78;
➔ // Manually cast double to int
➔ int intCastedValue = (int) doubleValue;
➔ // Print the int value after casting
➔ System.out.println("Explicit conversion from double to int: " +
intCastedValue);
➔
➔ // Another explicit conversion example
➔ // Declare a long variable
➔ long anotherLongValue = 1000L;
➔ // Manually cast long to short
➔ short shortCastedValue = (short) anotherLongValue;
➔ // Print the short value after casting
➔ System.out.println("Explicit conversion from long to short: " +
shortCastedValue);
➔ }
➔ }Casting between Primitive Types
➔ Casting: Converts one primitive type to another, useful for narrowing conversions.
➔ Syntax: Use (targetType) before the value to convert.
➔ Narrowing conversions: Larger data types to smaller ones, e.g., double to int.
➔ Casting a float to a byte can lead to data loss due to range limitations.
➔ Example: Casting a double to an int truncates the decimal part.
➔ // Importing necessary packages
➔ import java.util.*;
➔
➔ public class TypeCastingExample {
➔ public static void main(String[] args) {
➔ // Example of casting a double to an int
➔ double doubleValue = 9.99;
➔ // Casting double to int
➔ int intValue = (int) doubleValue;
➔ // Print the result of casting double to int
➔ System.out.println("Casting double to int: " + intValue);
➔ // Execute this line to see the output
➔
➔ // Example of casting a float to a byte
➔ float floatValue = 123.45f;
➔ // Casting float to byte
➔ byte byteValue = (byte) floatValue;
➔ // Print the result of casting float to byte
➔ System.out.println("Casting float to byte: " + byteValue);
➔ // Execute this line to see the output
➔ }
➔ }
Handling Data Loss During Casting
➔ Data loss is a major concern in narrowing conversions.
➔ Truncation occurs when converting floating-point numbers to integers, losing decimal parts.
➔ Overflow happens when values exceed the target type's range, causing unexpected results.
➔ Validate data before casting to prevent significant data loss or incorrect values.➔ // Example demonstrating type conversion pitfalls in Java
➔
➔ public class TypeConversionExample {
➔ public static void main(String[] args) {
➔ // Truncation example: converting a floating-point number to an integer
➔ double floatingPointNumber = 5.99;
➔ int truncatedNumber = (int) floatingPointNumber;
➔ // Output the result of truncation
➔ System.out.println("Truncated number: " + truncatedNumber); // Expected
output: 5
➔
➔ // Overflow example: converting a large integer to a byte
➔ int largeNumber = 130;
➔ byte overflowedByte = (byte) largeNumber;
➔ // Output the result of overflow
➔ System.out.println("Overflowed byte: " + overflowedByte); // Expected
output: -126
➔
➔ // Caution: Always validate data before casting
➔ // Example of checking range before casting
➔ if (largeNumber >= Byte.MIN_VALUE && largeNumber <= Byte.MAX_VALUE) {
➔ byte safeByte = (byte) largeNumber;
➔ System.out.println("Safely casted byte: " + safeByte);
➔ } else {
➔ System.out.println("Warning: Potential overflow detected!");
➔ }
➔ }
➔ }

Defining Constants with final Keyword➔ Constants in Java are defined using the 'final' keyword.
➔ A final variable's value cannot be changed once assigned.
➔ Syntax: final dataType variableName = value;
➔ Useful for fixed values like Pl, tax rates, etc.
➔ Enhances code integrity and readability.
➔ // Importing necessary packages
➔ import java.util.*;
➔
➔ // Main class
➔ public class ConstantsExample {
➔ // Defining a constant for PI using the 'final' keyword
➔ final double PI = 3.14159;
➔
➔ // Defining a constant for tax rate
➔ final double TAX_RATE = 0.07;
➔
➔ // Main method
➔ public static void main(String[] args) {
➔ // Creating an instance of the class to access non-static members
➔ ConstantsExample example = new ConstantsExample();
➔
➔ // Printing the value of PI
➔ System.out.println("The value of PI is: " + example.PI);
➔ // Execute the above line to see the output
➔
➔ // Printing the value of TAX_RATE
➔ System.out.println("The tax rate is: " + example.TAX_RATE);
➔ // Execute the above line to see the output
➔
➔ // Demonstrating the immutability of final variables
➔ // Uncommenting the following line will cause a compilation error
➔ // example.PI = 3.14;
➔ }
➔ }Benefits of Using Constants
➔ Constants enhance code readability by indicating unchangeable values.
➔ Prevent accidental modifications, reducing bugs and inconsistencies.
➔ Improve performance through compiler optimizations.
➔ Simplify updates by changing values in one place.
Scope and Visibility of Constants
➔ Constants can be declared at class level or within methods.
➔ Class-level constants are accessible throughout the class.
➔ Method-level constants are limited to the method scope.
➔ Use 'public', 'private', or 'protected' to control visibility.
➔ Class-level constants can be accessed by any method within the class.
➔ Method-level constants ensure values are confined to specific methods.
➔ Access modifiers manage whether constants are accessible outside the class.
➔ // Importing necessary packages
➔ import java.util.Scanner;
➔
➔ // Class to demonstrate the scope and visibility of constants in Java
➔ public class ConstantsDemo {
➔ // Class-level constant
➔ // This constant is accessible throughout the entire class
➔ public static final double PI = 3.14159;
➔
➔ // Private class-level constant
➔ // This constant is only accessible within this class
➔ private static final String SECRET_CODE = "XYZ123";
➔
➔ // Main method to run the program
➔ public static void main(String[] args) {
➔ // Method-level constant
➔ // This constant is only accessible within this method
➔ final int MAX_USERS = 100;
➔
➔ // Printing the class-level constant
➔ System.out.println("The value of PI is: " + PI);
➔ // Execute this line to see the output of the class-level constant
➔➔ // Printing the method-level constant
➔ System.out.println("The maximum number of users is: " + MAX_USERS);
➔ // Execute this line to see the output of the method-level constant
➔
➔ // Attempting to print the private class-level constant
➔ // This will work because we are within the same class
➔ System.out.println("The secret code is: " + SECRET_CODE);
➔ // Execute this line to see the output of the private class-level
constant
➔ }
➔
➔ // Another method to demonstrate class-level constant accessibility
➔ public void displayPI() {
➔ // Accessing the class-level constant PI
➔ System.out.println("Accessing PI from another method: " + PI);
➔ // Execute this line to see the output of accessing PI from another
method
➔ }
➔ }
Common Use Cases for Constants
➔ Mathematical constants like Pl and E are essential for precise calculations.
➔ Configuration settings such as file paths and URLs are defined as constants for consistency.
➔ Application-specific constants, like error codes, help manage application states.
➔ Constants ensure fixed values in algorithms remain unchanged and reliable.
➔ Using constants enhances code reliability, maintainability, and clarity.
Hands-on: Implementing Constants in Java
➔ Create a Java class with multiple constants to enhance code clarity.
➔ Use constants in methods for consistent and maintainable calculations.
➔ Experiment with access modifiers to control constant visibility.
➔ Understand how constants contribute to clean and efficient code.
➔ // Define a class to demonstrate the use of constants in Java
➔ public class ConstantsExample {
➔ // Define a public constant for the tax rate
➔ public static final double TAX_RATE = 0.07;➔
➔ // Define a private constant for the discount rate
➔ private static final double DISCOUNT_RATE = 0.10;
➔
➔ // Define a protected constant for the service charge
➔ protected static final double SERVICE_CHARGE = 0.05;
➔
➔ // Method to calculate the final price after tax
➔ public double calculateFinalPrice(double price) {
➔ // Calculate the final price by adding tax
➔ double finalPrice = price + (price * TAX_RATE);
➔ System.out.println("Final price after tax: " + finalPrice); // Output
the final price after tax
➔ return finalPrice;
➔ }
➔
➔ // Method to calculate the price after discount
➔ public double calculateDiscountedPrice(double price) {
➔ // Calculate the price after applying discount
➔ double discountedPrice = price - (price * DISCOUNT_RATE);
➔ System.out.println("Price after discount: " + discountedPrice); //
Output the price after discount
➔ return discountedPrice;
➔ }
➔
➔ // Method to calculate the total price including service charge
➔ public double calculateTotalWithServiceCharge(double price) {
➔ // Calculate the total price by adding service charge
➔ double totalPrice = price + (price * SERVICE_CHARGE);
➔ System.out.println("Total price with service charge: " + totalPrice);
// Output the total price with service charge
➔ return totalPrice;
➔ }➔
➔ // Main method to test the constants and methods
➔ public static void main(String[] args) {
➔ // Create an instance of ConstantsExample
➔ ConstantsExample example = new ConstantsExample();
➔
➔ // Test the calculateFinalPrice method
➔ example.calculateFinalPrice(100.0);
➔
➔ // Test the calculateDiscountedPrice method
➔ example.calculateDiscountedPrice(100.0);
➔
➔ // Test the calculateTotalWithServiceCharge method
➔ example.calculateTotalWithServiceCharge(100.0);
➔ }
➔ }
Introduction to the Scanner class
➔ Scanner class is part of the java.util package.
➔ Widely used for reading input from sources like keyboard and files.
➔ Simplifies parsing of primitive types and strings using regular expressions.
➔ Essential for handling user input in Java applications.
➔ Facilitates easy input handling, crucial for interactive programs.
➔ // Java program to demonstrate basic input and output using the Scanner class
➔ import java.util.Scanner;
➔
➔ public class BasicInputOutput {
➔ public static void main(String[] args) {
➔ // Create a Scanner object to read input
➔ Scanner scanner = new Scanner("25\nJohn\n3.14"); // Simulated input
➔
➔ // Read an integer from the input
➔ int age = scanner.nextInt();
➔ System.out.println("Age: " + age); // Output the integer➔
➔ // Read a string from the input
➔ String name = scanner.next();
➔ System.out.println("Name: " + name); // Output the string
➔
➔ // Read a double from the input
➔ double piValue = scanner.nextDouble();
➔ System.out.println("Value of Pi: " + piValue); // Output the double
➔
➔ // Close the scanner
➔ scanner.close();
➔ }
➔ }
Reading input from the console
➔ Scanner class offers methods to read various data types.
➔ Use nextInt() for integers, nextDouble() for doubles.
➔ nextLine() captures entire lines, including spaces.
➔ Other methods read tokens separated by whitespace.
➔ Flexibility in input handling enhances program interactivity.
➔ // This Java program demonstrates the use of the Scanner class to read
different types of input.
➔ // It simulates user input by using a predefined string.
➔
➔ import java.util.Scanner;
➔
➔ public class InputExample {
➔ public static void main(String[] args) {
➔ // Simulate user input using a predefined string
➔ String input = "42 3.14 Hello World";
➔ Scanner scanner = new Scanner(input);
➔
➔ // Read an integer from the input
➔ int intValue = scanner.nextInt();➔ System.out.println("Integer value: " + intValue); // Output: Integer
value: 42
➔
➔ // Read a double from the input
➔ double doubleValue = scanner.nextDouble();
➔ System.out.println("Double value: " + doubleValue); // Output: Double
value: 3.14
➔
➔ // Read the rest of the line as a string
➔ // This will work correctly as there are no remaining lines in the
simulated input
➔ String line = scanner.next(); // Read the next word "Hello"
➔ line += " " + scanner.nextLine(); // Continue to read until the end of
input
➔ System.out.println("Line: " + line); // Output: Line: Hello World
➔
➔ // Close the scanner
➔ scanner.close();
➔ }
➔ }
➔
➔ // This program demonstrates how the Scanner class can be used to read
different types of data from input.
➔ // It shows the use of nextInt(), nextDouble(), and nextLine() methods to
capture integer, double, and string inputs respectively.
Handling different input types
➔ Scanner class can parse integers, doubles, strings, and more.
➔ Use hasNextInt() to check if the next input is an integer.
➔ Preemptive input type checks prevent runtime errors.
➔ Use hasNextDouble() to verify if the next input is a double.
➔ Exception handling is crucial for managing invalid inputs.
➔ Robust exception handling enhances program reliability and user experience.
➔ // Importing the Scanner class from the java.util package➔ import java.util.Scanner;
➔
➔ public class ScannerExample {
➔ public static void main(String[] args) {
➔ // Creating a Scanner object to read input from the console
➔ Scanner scanner = new Scanner(System.in);
➔
➔ // Prompting the user to enter an integer
➔ System.out.println("Please enter an integer:");
➔
➔ // Checking if the next input is an integer
➔ if (scanner.hasNextInt()) {
➔ // Reading the integer input
➔ int userInt = scanner.nextInt();
➔ // Printing the integer input
➔ System.out.println("You entered the integer: " + userInt);
➔ } else {
➔ // Handling the case where the input is not an integer
➔ System.out.println("That's not a valid integer!");
➔ }
➔
➔ // Clearing the scanner buffer
➔ scanner.nextLine();
➔
➔ // Prompting the user to enter a double
➔ System.out.println("Please enter a double:");
➔
➔ // Checking if the next input is a double
➔ if (scanner.hasNextDouble()) {
➔ // Reading the double input
➔ double userDouble = scanner.nextDouble();
➔ // Printing the double input
➔ System.out.println("You entered the double: " + userDouble);➔ } else {
➔ // Handling the case where the input is not a double
➔ System.out.println("That's not a valid double!");
➔ }
➔
➔ // Closing the scanner to free up resources
➔ scanner.close();
➔ }
➔ }
Common input/output errors
➔ Mismatch Exception occurs when input type doesn't match the expected type.
➔ NoSuchElementException is thrown when no input is available to read.
➔ IllegalStateException occurs if a closed Scanner is used.
➔ Handling these exceptions is crucial for robust Java programs.
➔ // Importing necessary classes for input handling
➔ import java.util.InputMismatchException;
➔ import java.util.NoSuchElementException;
➔ import java.util.Scanner;
➔
➔ public class InputOutputHandling {
➔ public static void main(String[] args) {
➔ // Creating a Scanner object to read input from the user
➔ Scanner scanner = new Scanner(System.in);
➔
➔ try {
➔ // Prompting the user to enter an integer
➔ System.out.println("Please enter an integer:");
➔ // Attempting to read an integer from the user
➔ int number = scanner.nextInt();
➔ // Printing the entered integer
➔ System.out.println("You entered: " + number);
➔ } catch (InputMismatchException e) {
➔ // Handling the case where the input is not an integer➔ System.out.println("Error: You must enter an integer.");
➔ } catch (NoSuchElementException e) {
➔ // Handling the case where no input is available
➔ System.out.println("Error: No input was found.");
➔ } catch (IllegalStateException e) {
➔ // Handling the case where the scanner is closed
➔ System.out.println("Error: Scanner is closed.");
➔ } finally {
➔ // Closing the scanner to free up resources
➔ scanner.close();
➔ }
➔ }
➔ }
Hands-on: Building interactive programs
➔ Combine the Scanner class with loops to enhance user interaction.
➔ Use loops to continuously prompt users for input.
➔ Implement conditionals to make decisions based on user input.
➔ Create a simple interactive console application using these concepts.
➔ Enhance user experience by dynamically responding to input.
➔ // This Java program demonstrates the use of the Scanner class with loops and
conditionals
➔ // to create an interactive console application.
➔
➔ import java.util.Scanner;
➔
➔ public class InteractiveProgram {
➔ public static void main(String[] args) {
➔ // Create a Scanner object to read input
➔ Scanner scanner = new Scanner("yes\nno\nexit\n"); // Simulated input
➔
➔ // Variable to store user input
➔ String userInput;
➔➔ // Start a loop to continuously prompt the user
➔ while (true) {
➔ System.out.println("Do you want to continue? (yes/no/exit)");
➔ userInput = scanner.nextLine();
➔
➔ // Use conditionals to respond to user input
➔ if (userInput.equalsIgnoreCase("yes")) {
➔ System.out.println("Great! Let's continue."); // Output for
'yes'
➔ } else if (userInput.equalsIgnoreCase("no")) {
➔ System.out.println("Okay, taking a break."); // Output for 'no'
➔ } else if (userInput.equalsIgnoreCase("exit")) {
➔ System.out.println("Exiting the program. Goodbye!"); // Output
for 'exit'
➔ break; // Exit the loop
➔ } else {
➔ System.out.println("Invalid input. Please type 'yes', 'no', or
'exit'."); // Output for invalid input
➔ }
➔ }
➔
➔ // Close the scanner
➔ scanner.close();
➔ }
➔ }
Control Flow – Conditions and Loops
Introduction to Relational Operators
➔ Relational operators are key for comparing two values in programming.
➔ Used in scenarios like sorting, access control, and more.
➔ They return a boolean result: true or false, aiding decision-making.
➔ Essential for controlling program flow through conditional statements.
➔ Understanding them is crucial for writing efficient code.
➔ // Java program to demonstrate relational and logical operators➔
➔ public class RelationalLogicalOperators {
➔ public static void main(String[] args) {
➔ // Relational Operators Example
➔ int age = 20;
➔ int requiredAge = 18;
➔
➔ // Check if age is greater than or equal to requiredAge
➔ boolean isOldEnough = age >= requiredAge;
➔ System.out.println("Is the user old enough? " + isOldEnough); // true
➔
➔ // Logical Operators Example
➔ boolean hasPermission = true;
➔
➔ // Check if the user is old enough and has permission
➔ boolean canAccessFeature = isOldEnough && hasPermission;
➔ System.out.println("Can the user access the feature? " +
canAccessFeature); // true
➔
➔ // Check if the user is not old enough or does not have permission
➔ boolean cannotAccessFeature = !isOldEnough || !hasPermission;
➔ System.out.println("Can the user not access the feature? " +
cannotAccessFeature); // false
➔
➔ // Relational Operators with equals and not equals
➔ int number1 = 10;
➔ int number2 = 20;
➔
➔ // Check if two numbers are equal
➔ boolean areNumbersEqual = number1 == number2;
➔ System.out.println("Are the numbers equal? " + areNumbersEqual); //
false
➔➔ // Check if two numbers are not equal
➔ boolean areNumbersNotEqual = number1 != number2;
➔ System.out.println("Are the numbers not equal? " + areNumbersNotEqual);
// true
➔ }
➔ }
Using ==, !=, >, <, >=, <=
➔ 0 '==' checks if two values are equal, useful for verifying equality.
➔ '!='checks if two values are not equal, ensuring they differ.
➔ '>' and '<' compare values to determine greater or lesser.
➔ '>=' and '<=' check for greater or equal, lesser or equal.
➔ Relational operators are crucial for precise comparisons in code.
➔ // Importing necessary packages
➔ import java.util.Arrays;
➔
➔ public class RelationalOperatorsExample {
➔ public static void main(String[] args) {
➔ // Initializing variables for comparison
➔ int a = 10;
➔ int b = 20;
➔ int c = 10;
➔
➔ // Using '==' operator to check equality
➔ System.out.println("Is a equal to b? " + (a == b));
➔ // Execute this line to see if a is equal to b
➔
➔ // Using '!=' operator to check inequality
➔ System.out.println("Is a not equal to b? " + (a != b));
➔ // Execute this line to see if a is not equal to b
➔
➔ // Using '>' operator to check if one value is greater than another
➔ System.out.println("Is b greater than a? " + (b > a));
➔ // Execute this line to see if b is greater than a➔
➔ // Using '<' operator to check if one value is less than another
➔ System.out.println("Is a less than b? " + (a < b));
➔ // Execute this line to see if a is less than b
➔
➔ // Using '>=' operator to check if one value is greater than or equal
to another
➔ System.out.println("Is a greater than or equal to c? " + (a >= c));
➔ // Execute this line to see if a is greater than or equal to c
➔
➔ // Using '<=' operator to check if one value is less than or equal to
another
➔ System.out.println("Is b less than or equal to c? " + (b <= c));
➔ // Execute this line to see if b is less than or equal to c
➔
➔ // Example of using relational operators in an array sorting context
➔ int[] numbers = {3, 5, 1, 4, 2};
➔ Arrays.sort(numbers);
➔ System.out.println("Sorted array: " + Arrays.toString(numbers));
➔ // Execute this line to see the sorted array
➔ }
➔ }
Logical Operators: &&, ||, !
➔ '&&' is the logical AND operator, true if both operands are true.
➔ '' is the logical OR operator, true if at least one operand is true.
➔ '!' is the logical NOT operator, inverts the boolean value.
➔ Logical operators are key for building complex conditional statements.
➔ // Importing necessary packages
➔ import java.util.*;
➔
➔ public class LogicalOperatorsExample {
➔ public static void main(String[] args) {
➔ // Example 1: Using logical AND operator➔ boolean isAdult = true;
➔ boolean hasTicket = true;
➔ // Check if both conditions are true
➔ boolean canEnter = isAdult && hasTicket;
➔ // Print the result
➔ System.out.println("Can enter the event: " + canEnter); // Execute this
line to see the output
➔
➔ // Example 2: Using logical OR operator
➔ boolean isMember = false;
➔ boolean hasInvitation = true;
➔ // Check if at least one condition is true
➔ boolean canAttend = isMember || hasInvitation;
➔ // Print the result
➔ System.out.println("Can attend the party: " + canAttend); // Execute
this line to see the output
➔
➔ // Example 3: Using logical NOT operator
➔ boolean isRaining = false;
➔ // Invert the boolean value
➔ boolean goForWalk = !isRaining;
➔ // Print the result
➔ System.out.println("Can go for a walk: " + goForWalk); // Execute this
line to see the output
➔ }
➔ }
Combining Relational and Logical Operators
➔ Combining relational and logical operators enhances code complexity and functionality.
➔ Allows simultaneous evaluation of multiple criteria in decision-making.
➔ Useful in if statements and loops for evaluating multiple conditions.
➔ Enhances program robustness and flexibility by handling complex scenarios.
➔ Prepares for understanding operator precedence and associativity in expressions.
➔ // Example demonstrating the use of relational and logical operators in Java➔ public class LogicalOperatorsExample {
➔ public static void main(String[] args) {
➔ // Define user status and permissions
➔ boolean isLoggedIn = true;
➔ boolean hasPermission = false;
➔ int userAge = 20;
➔
➔ // Check if the user is logged in and has permission
➔ if (isLoggedIn && hasPermission) {
➔ System.out.println("User is logged in and has permission."); //
This will not execute
➔ } else {
➔ System.out.println("User does not have permission or is not logged
in."); // This will execute
➔ }
➔
➔ // Check if the user is either logged in or has permission
➔ if (isLoggedIn || hasPermission) {
➔ System.out.println("User is either logged in or has permission.");
// This will execute
➔ }
➔
➔ // Check if the user is logged in and is above 18 years old
➔ if (isLoggedIn && userAge > 18) {
➔ System.out.println("User is logged in and is above 18."); // This
will execute
➔ }
➔
➔ // Check if the user is not logged in or does not have permission
➔ if (!isLoggedIn || !hasPermission) {
➔ System.out.println("User is not logged in or does not have
permission."); // This will execute
➔ }➔ }
➔ }
Operator Precedence and Associativity
➔ Operator precedence determines the order of evaluation in expressions.
➔ Associativity defines the direction of evaluation for operators with the same precedence.
➔ Left-to-right associativity is common for most operators.
➔ Parentheses can override default precedence to control evaluation order.
➔ Using parentheses ensures specific parts of an expression are evaluated first.
➔ // This Java code demonstrates operator precedence and associativity
➔
➔ public class OperatorPrecedence {
➔ public static void main(String[] args) {
➔ // Example 1: Demonstrating precedence
➔ int result1 = 5 + 3 * 2; // Multiplication has higher precedence than
addition
➔ System.out.println("Result of 5 + 3 * 2: " + result1); // Execute to
see the output
➔
➔ // Example 2: Demonstrating associativity
➔ int result2 = 5 - 3 + 2; // Both subtraction and addition have the same
precedence
➔ System.out.println("Result of 5 - 3 + 2: " + result2); // Execute to
see the output
➔
➔ // Example 3: Using parentheses to override precedence
➔ int result3 = (5 + 3) * 2; // Parentheses change the order of
evaluation
➔ System.out.println("Result of (5 + 3) * 2: " + result3); // Execute to
see the output
➔
➔ // Example 4: Complex expression with multiple operators
➔ int result4 = 10 - 2 + 3 * 4 / 2; // Default precedence and
associativity➔ System.out.println("Result of 10 - 2 + 3 * 4 / 2: " + result4); //
Execute to see the output
➔
➔ // Example 5: Using parentheses in a complex expression
➔ int result5 = 10 - (2 + 3) * (4 / 2); // Parentheses change the order
of evaluation
➔ System.out.println("Result of 10 - (2 + 3) * (4 / 2): " + result5); //
Execute to see the output
➔ }
➔ }
Common Mistakes with Logical Operators
➔ Misusing '&&' and '||' can lead to tricky logic errors.
➔ Incorrect use of '&&' or '||' changes condition outcomes.
➔ Parentheses are crucial for correct evaluation order.
➔ Forgetting parentheses can lead to unexpected logic.
➔ Confusing '=' with '==' in conditions is a common error.
➔ Assignment instead of comparison can cause subtle bugs.
➔ Awareness of these pitfalls leads to more reliable code.
➔ // This code demonstrates common pitfalls with logical operators in Java
➔ public class LogicalOperatorsExample {
➔ public static void main(String[] args) {
➔ // Example 1: Misusing '&&' and '||'
➔ boolean isRaining = true;
➔ boolean hasUmbrella = false;
➔ // Correct use of '||' to check if either condition is true
➔ if (isRaining || hasUmbrella) {
➔ System.out.println("You can go outside."); // Expected output
➔ } else {
➔ System.out.println("You should stay inside.");
➔ }
➔
➔ // Example 2: Importance of parentheses
➔ boolean isWeekend = true;
➔ boolean isHoliday = false;➔ int hour = 10;
➔ // Correct use of parentheses to ensure the intended order of
evaluation
➔ if ((isWeekend || isHoliday) && hour < 12) {
➔ System.out.println("You can sleep in."); // Expected output
➔ } else {
➔ System.out.println("Time to get up!");
➔ }
➔
➔ // Example 3: Confusing '=' with '=='
➔ int a = 5;
➔ int b = 5;
➔ // Correct use of '==' to compare values
➔ if (a == b) {
➔ System.out.println("a and b are equal."); // Expected output
➔ } else {
➔ System.out.println("a and b are not equal.");
➔ }
➔ }
➔ }

➔ Test and enhance skills in writing and debugging Java expressions.

➔ // Java program to demonstrate the use of relational and logical operators
➔ public class LogicalOperatorsExample {
➔ public static void main(String[] args) {
➔ // Declare and initialize variables
➔ int a = 10;
➔ int b = 20;
➔ int c = 30;➔
➔ // Evaluate a complex expression using relational and logical operators
➔ // Check if a is less than b and b is less than c
➔ boolean result1 = (a < b) && (b < c);
➔ System.out.println("Is 'a' less than 'b' and 'b' less than 'c'? : " +
result1); // true
➔
➔ // Check if a is greater than b or b is less than c
➔ boolean result2 = (a > b) || (b < c);
➔ System.out.println("Is 'a' greater than 'b' or 'b' less than 'c'? : " +
result2); // true
➔
➔ // Check if a is not equal to b and b is equal to c
➔ boolean result3 = (a != b) && (b == c);
➔ System.out.println("Is 'a' not equal to 'b' and 'b' equal to 'c'? : " +
result3); // false
➔
➔ // Check if a is less than b or b is greater than or equal to c
➔ boolean result4 = (a < b) || (b >= c);
➔ System.out.println("Is 'a' less than 'b' or 'b' greater than or equal
to 'c'? : " + result4); // true
➔ }
➔ }
