• }• }
Common Errors with Static and Instance Usage
• Misusing static for instance-specific data can cause shared state issues.
• Static methods cannot access instance members, leading to errors.
• Forgetting to use the class name for static members causes confusion.
• Understanding these errors helps in writing efficient and error-free code.
• // This code example demonstrates the correct usage of static and instance
members in Java.
• // It highlights common pitfalls and how to avoid them.
•
• public class StaticVsInstanceDemo {
• // Static variable shared across all instances
• private static int staticCounter = 0;
•
• // Instance variable unique to each instance
• private int instanceCounter = 0;
•
• // Static method to access static variable
• public static void incrementStaticCounter() {
• staticCounter++;
• System.out.println("Static Counter: " + staticCounter); // Outputs the
shared static counter
• }
•
• // Instance method to access instance variable
• public void incrementInstanceCounter() {
• instanceCounter++;
• System.out.println("Instance Counter: " + instanceCounter); // Outputs
the instance-specific counter
• }
•
• public static void main(String[] args) {
• // Demonstrating static member access• StaticVsInstanceDemo.incrementStaticCounter(); // Correctly using class
name to access static method
• StaticVsInstanceDemo.incrementStaticCounter();
•
• // Creating instances to demonstrate instance member access
• StaticVsInstanceDemo obj1 = new StaticVsInstanceDemo();
• StaticVsInstanceDemo obj2 = new StaticVsInstanceDemo();
•
• // Incrementing instance counters
• obj1.incrementInstanceCounter(); // Outputs: Instance Counter: 1
• obj2.incrementInstanceCounter(); // Outputs: Instance Counter: 1
•
• // Incrementing static counter from an instance (not recommended)
• obj1.incrementStaticCounter(); // Outputs: Static Counter: 3
• // This works but is not recommended as it can lead to confusion
•
• // Demonstrating the error of accessing instance members from a static
context
• // Uncommenting the following line will cause a compilation error
• // incrementInstanceCounter(); // Error: non-static method cannot be
referenced from a static context
• }
• }
Understanding Inheritance in Java
• Inheritance allows a subclass to inherit fields and methods from a superclass.
• Promotes code reusability by building on existing class structures.
• Establishes a hierarchical relationship between classes, similar to a family tree.
• Enables the creation of specialized versions of existing classes.
• Leads to more organized, manageable, and efficient code.
Syntax of the extends Keyword
• The 'extends' keyword establishes inheritance between classes.
• Syntax: 'class SubclassName extends SuperclassName'.
• Creates a parent-child relationship between superclass and subclass.
• Enables the subclass to inherit fields and methods from the superclass.• Facilitates code reuse and hierarchical class structures.
• Allows for specialization and refinement of existing classes.
• // This code example demonstrates the use of the 'extends' keyword in Java to
establish inheritance.
•
• // Main class to execute the code
• public class InheritanceExample {
• public static void main(String[] args) {
• // Create an instance of Dog
• Dog dog = new Dog();
• // Call the makeSound method on Dog instance
• dog.makeSound(); // Output: Dog barks
•
• // Create an instance of Cat
• Cat cat = new Cat();
• // Call the makeSound method on Cat instance
• cat.makeSound(); // Output: Cat meows
•
• // Create an instance of Animal
• Animal genericAnimal = new Animal();
• // Call the makeSound method on Animal instance
• genericAnimal.makeSound(); // Output: Animal makes a sound
• }
• }
•
• // Superclass
• class Animal {
• // Method in the superclass
• public void makeSound() {
• System.out.println("Animal makes a sound"); // Default sound for any
animal
• }
• }•
• // Subclass that extends the superclass
• class Dog extends Animal {
• // Overriding the makeSound method in the subclass
• @Override
• public void makeSound() {
• System.out.println("Dog barks"); // Specific sound for a dog
• }
• }
•
• // Subclass that extends the superclass
• class Cat extends Animal {
• // Overriding the makeSound method in the subclass
• @Override
• public void makeSound() {
• System.out.println("Cat meows"); // Specific sound for a cat
• }
• }
Creating a Subclass in Java
• Define a subclass using the 'extends' keyword to inherit from a superclass.
• Subclass inherits all non-private members, promoting code reuse.
• Introduce new fields and methods specific to the subclass.
• Create specialized classes that build on general class functionality.
• Example: Subclass 'Car' inherits from 'Vehicle' and adds unique methods.
• // This code demonstrates the use of the 'extends' keyword to create a subclass
in Java.
• // We have a superclass 'Vehicle' and a subclass 'Car' that inherits from
'Vehicle'.
•
• // Main class containing the main method
• public class Main {
• public static void main(String[] args) {
• // Create an instance of Car• Car myCar = new Car();
•
• // Call methods from the superclass
• myCar.start(); // Output: Vehicle is starting.
• myCar.stop(); // Output: Vehicle is stopping.
•
• // Call method from the subclass
• myCar.openSunroof(); // Output: Sunroof is open.
• }
• }
•
• // Superclass
• class Vehicle {
• // Method to start the vehicle
• public void start() {
• System.out.println("Vehicle is starting.");
• }
•
• // Method to stop the vehicle
• public void stop() {
• System.out.println("Vehicle is stopping.");
• }
• }
•
• // Subclass that extends Vehicle
• class Car extends Vehicle {
• // Method specific to Car
• public void openSunroof() {
• System.out.println("Sunroof is open.");
• }
• }
Accessing Superclass Members• Subclass can access public and protected members of the superclass, enabling code reuse.
• The 'super' keyword is used to access superclass constructors and methods.
• Private members of the superclass remain inaccessible to the subclass, ensuring
encapsulation.
• Encapsulation hides internal implementation details, promoting clean and maintainable
code.
• Inheritance respects encapsulation, maintaining class integrity and independence.
• // This code example demonstrates inheritance in Java, focusing on how a
subclass interacts with its superclass.
• // It shows the use of the 'super' keyword to access superclass constructors
and methods.
•
• // Main class containing the main method
• public class InheritanceExample {
• public static void main(String[] args) {
• // Create an instance of the subclass
• Dog myDog = new Dog("Buddy", "Golden Retriever");
•
• // Call methods from both the superclass and subclass
• myDog.displayInfo(); // Calls the method from the superclass
• myDog.bark(); // Calls the method from the subclass
• }
• }
•
• // Superclass
• class Animal {
• // Protected member, accessible by subclasses
• protected String name;
•
• // Constructor of the superclass
• public Animal(String name) {
• this.name = name;
• }
•
• // Method of the superclass• public void displayInfo() {
• System.out.println("Animal Name: " + name); // Outputs the name of the
animal
• }
• }
•
• // Subclass extending the superclass
• class Dog extends Animal {
• // Private member, not accessible by subclasses
• private String breed;
•
• // Constructor of the subclass
• public Dog(String name, String breed) {
• super(name); // Calls the constructor of the superclass
• this.breed = breed;
• }
•
• // Method of the subclass
• public void bark() {
• System.out.println(name + " is barking!"); // Outputs a message
indicating the dog is barking
• }
•
• // Overriding the method from the superclass
• @Override
• public void displayInfo() {
• super.displayInfo(); // Calls the method from the superclass
• System.out.println("Breed: " + breed); // Outputs the breed of the dog
• }
• }
Inheritance Hierarchies
• Inheritance hierarchies in Java include single, multilevel, and hierarchical forms.• Single inheritance: a subclass inherits from one superclass, extending its functionality.
• Multilevel inheritance: a chain where a subclass becomes a superclass for another subclass.
• Hierarchical inheritance: multiple subclasses inherit from a single superclass, sharing
common functionality.
• These hierarchies enable complex class structures and promote code reuse.
• // This code demonstrates different types of inheritance hierarchies in Java.
•
• public class InheritanceDemo {
• public static void main(String[] args) {
• // Demonstrating Single Inheritance
• Dog dog = new Dog();
• dog.eat(); // Inherited from Animal
• dog.bark(); // Defined in Dog
•
• // Demonstrating Multilevel Inheritance
• Puppy puppy = new Puppy();
• puppy.eat(); // Inherited from Animal
• puppy.bark(); // Inherited from Dog
• puppy.weep(); // Defined in Puppy
•
• // Demonstrating Hierarchical Inheritance
• Cat cat = new Cat();
• cat.eat(); // Inherited from Animal
• cat.meow(); // Defined in Cat
• }
• }
•
• // Single Inheritance Example
• class Animal {
• void eat() {
• System.out.println("Animal eats");
• }
• }
•• class Dog extends Animal { // Dog is a subclass of Animal
• void bark() {
• System.out.println("Dog barks");
• }
• }
•
• // Multilevel Inheritance Example
• class Puppy extends Dog { // Puppy is a subclass of Dog
• void weep() {
• System.out.println("Puppy weeps");
• }
• }
•
• // Hierarchical Inheritance Example
• class Cat extends Animal { // Cat is another subclass of Animal
• void meow() {
• System.out.println("Cat meows");
• }
• }
Benefits of Using Inheritance
• Inheritance promotes code reusability by allowing subclasses to inherit and extend existing
classes.
• Reduces redundancy and ensures consistency across the codebase.
• Facilitates maintenance and scalability by centralizing core functionality in superclasses.
• Enables easy addition of new features with minimal changes to existing code.
• Supports polymorphic behavior, allowing objects to be treated as instances of their
superclass.
• Enhances code flexibility and adaptability, making it more robust.
Common Mistakes in Inheritance
• Overusing inheritance can create complex and rigid class hierarchies.
• Misunderstanding access levels can lead to access issues between classes.
• Inheritance should be used only when there is a clear 'is-a' relationship.
• Ignoring the 'is-a' relationship can complicate the codebase.
Hands-on: Implementing Basic Inheritance• Create a superclass with fields and methods as a foundation.
• Use the 'extends' keyword to create a subclass inheriting from the superclass.
• Implement additional methods in the subclass to extend its functionality.
• Test the subclass to ensure it inherits and extends the superclass functionality.
• Understand the practical application of inheritance in Java.
• // This Java program demonstrates basic inheritance.
• // We will create a superclass called 'Animal' and a subclass called 'Dog'.
•
• // Main class containing the main method
• public class InheritanceExample {
• public static void main(String[] args) {
• // Create an instance of the Dog class
• Dog myDog = new Dog("Buddy", 5);
•
• // Call methods from the superclass and subclass
• myDog.displayInfo(); // Method inherited from Animal
• myDog.bark(); // Method specific to Dog
• }
• }
•
• // Superclass
• class Animal {
• // Fields of the superclass
• private String name;
• private int age;
•
• // Constructor of the superclass
• public Animal(String name, int age) {
• this.name = name;
• this.age = age;
• }
•
• // Method of the superclass
• public void displayInfo() {• System.out.println("Animal Name: " + name);
• System.out.println("Animal Age: " + age);
• }
• }
•
• // Subclass that extends the superclass
• class Dog extends Animal {
• // Constructor of the subclass
• public Dog(String name, int age) {
• // Call the constructor of the superclass
• super(name, age);
• }
•
• // Method specific to the subclass
• public void bark() {
• System.out.println("Woof! Woof!");
• }
• }
•
• /*
• Output:
• Animal Name: Buddy
• Animal Age: 5
• Woof! Woof!
• */
•
• // In this example, the Dog class inherits the properties and methods of the
Animal class.
• // The Dog class also has its own method, bark(), which is not present in the
Animal class.
• // This demonstrates how inheritance allows us to extend the functionality of a
superclass.Concept of Method Overriding
• Method overriding allows a subclass to implement a specific version of a method from its
superclass.
• The overridden method must have the same name, return type, and parameters as the
superclass method.
• Enables runtime polymorphism, where the method executed is determined by the object's
actual type.
• Facilitates dynamic method dispatch, allowing subclasses to modify or extend inherited
methods.
• class Animal {
• void sound() {
• System.out.println("Animal makes a sound");
• }
• }
•
• class Dog extends Animal {
• @Override
• void sound() {
• System.out.println("Dog barks");
• }
• }
•
• public class Main {
• public static void main(String[] args) {
• Animal myAnimal = new Animal(); // Animal reference and object
• Animal myDog = new Dog(); // Animal reference but Dog object
•
• myAnimal.sound(); // Output: Animal makes a sound
• myDog.sound(); // Output: Dog barks
• }
• }
Rules for Overriding Methods
• Overriding methods must have the same name, return type, and parameters as the
superclass method.• The access level of the overriding method cannot be more restrictive than the superclass
method.
• Methods marked as final or static cannot be overridden.
• These rules ensure compatibility and maintain the integrity of the overridden method.
• public class MethodOverridingExample {
• public static void main(String[] args) {
• // Create an instance of the subclass
• Subclass subclassInstance = new Subclass();
•
• // Call the overridden method
• subclassInstance.display(); // Output: "Display method in Subclass"
•
• // Call the superclass method using super
• subclassInstance.callSuperDisplay(); // Output: "Display method in
Superclass"
• }
• }
•
• // Superclass definition
• class Superclass {
• // A method that can be overridden
• public void display() {
• System.out.println("Display method in Superclass");
• }
• }
•
• // Subclass definition
• class Subclass extends Superclass {
• // Overriding the display method
• @Override
• public void display() {
• System.out.println("Display method in Subclass");
• }
•• // Method to demonstrate calling the superclass method
• public void callSuperDisplay() {
• super.display(); // Calls the display method in Superclass
• }
• }
•
• /*
• Key Points:
• 1. The `display` method in `Subclass` has the same name, return type, and
parameters as in `Superclass`.
• 2. The access level of the `display` method in `Subclass` is not more
restrictive than in `Superclass`.
• 3. The `super` keyword is used to call the superclass version of the overridden
method.
• */
Using the super Keyword
• The super keyword acts as a reference to the immediate parent class object.
• Essential for calling overridden methods from the superclass.
• Allows access to superclass constructors for initializing inherited fields.
• Differentiates between methods in superclass and subclass with the same name.
• Facilitates integration of superclass functionality with subclass enhancements.
• // This code example demonstrates the use of the 'super' keyword in Java
• // to call a method from the superclass that has been overridden in the
subclass.
•
• // Main class containing the main method
• public class SuperKeywordExample {
• public static void main(String[] args) {
• // Create an instance of the subclass
• Dog dog = new Dog();
•
• // Call the overridden method
• dog.displayInfo(); // This will call the overridden method in Dog class• }
• }
•
• // Superclass
• class Animal {
• // Method to be overridden
• public void displayInfo() {
• System.out.println("I am an animal.");
• }
• }
•
• // Subclass
• class Dog extends Animal {
• // Overriding the displayInfo method
• @Override
• public void displayInfo() {
• // Call the displayInfo method of the superclass using 'super'
• super.displayInfo(); // Calls the method from Animal class
•
• // Additional functionality in the subclass
• System.out.println("I am a dog.");
• }
• }
•
• /*
• Output:
• I am an animal.
• I am a dog.
•
• Explanation:
• - The 'super.displayInfo()' call in the Dog class's displayInfo method
• invokes the displayInfo method from the Animal class.
• - This demonstrates how 'super' is used to access the superclass's method• even when it is overridden in the subclass.
• */
Accessing Superclass Methods
• The super keyword allows access to superclass methods in Java.
• Essential for invoking overridden methods in the superclass.
• Preserves the original behavior of the superclass method.
• Maintains class hierarchy integrity and ensures necessary operations.
• Facilitates integration of superclass method functionality with subclass enhancements.
• // This code demonstrates the use of the 'super' keyword to access a superclass
method
• // that has been overridden in a subclass.
•
• // Main class containing the main method
• public class SuperKeywordExample {
• public static void main(String[] args) {
• // Create an instance of the subclass
• Dog dog = new Dog();
• // Call the overridden method
• dog.makeSound(); // Output: Animal makes sound. Dog barks.
• }
• }
•
• // Superclass
• class Animal {
• // Method to be overridden
• public void makeSound() {
• System.out.println("Animal makes sound.");
• }
• }
•
• // Subclass that extends the superclass
• class Dog extends Animal {
• // Overriding the makeSound method• @Override
• public void makeSound() {
• // Call the superclass method using 'super'
• super.makeSound(); // Calls Animal's makeSound method
• // Additional behavior specific to Dog
• System.out.println("Dog barks.");
• }
• }
•
• /*
• Explanation:
• - We have a superclass `Animal` with a method `makeSound`.
• - The `Dog` class extends `Animal` and overrides the `makeSound` method.
• - Inside the overridden method in `Dog`, we use `super.makeSound()` to call the
original method from `Animal`.
• - This allows us to add new behavior ("Dog barks.") while preserving the
original behavior ("Animal makes sound.").
• */
Overriding vs Overloading
• Overriding redefines a method in a subclass with the same signature as in the superclass.
• Overriding is essential for runtime polymorphism, allowing dynamic method selection.
• Overloading creates multiple methods with the same name but different parameters in the
same class.
• Overloading is a compile-time concept, enhancing program readability and usability.
• Both concepts involve methods with similar names but serve different purposes.
• // This code example demonstrates method overriding and overloading in Java.
•
• // Main class containing the main method
• public class MethodExample {
• public static void main(String[] args) {
• // Demonstrating method overriding
• Animal myDog = new Dog(); // Upcasting
• myDog.makeSound(); // Calls the overridden method in Dog class•
• // Demonstrating method overloading
• Calculator calculator = new Calculator();
• System.out.println("Sum of two integers: " + calculator.add(5, 10)); //
Calls add(int, int)
• System.out.println("Sum of three integers: " + calculator.add(5, 10,
15)); // Calls add(int, int, int)
• System.out.println("Sum of two doubles: " + calculator.add(5.5, 10.5));
// Calls add(double, double)
• }
• }
•
• // Superclass
• class Animal {
• // Method to be overridden
• public void makeSound() {
• System.out.println("Animal makes a sound");
• }
• }
•
• // Subclass that overrides the makeSound method
• class Dog extends Animal {
• // Overriding the makeSound method
• @Override
• public void makeSound() {
• System.out.println("Dog barks");
• }
• }
•
• // Class demonstrating method overloading
• class Calculator {
• // Overloaded method with two integer parameters
• public int add(int a, int b) {• return a + b;
• }
•
• // Overloaded method with three integer parameters
• public int add(int a, int b, int c) {
• return a + b + c;
• }
•
• // Overloaded method with two double parameters
• public double add(double a, double b) {
• return a + b;
• }
• }
Importance of Overriding to Polymorphism
• Overriding is key to achieving runtime polymorphism in Java.
• Enables dynamic method dispatch based on actual object type.
• Allows objects to be treated as instances of their superclass, enhancing flexibility.
• Crucial for implementing interface methods in subclasses, ensuring specific behavior
execution.
• Facilitates writing generic and reusable code through polymorphism.
• // This code demonstrates method overriding and polymorphism in Java.
• // We have a superclass Animal with a method sound(), which is overridden in
subclasses Dog and Cat.
• // The main method shows how polymorphism allows us to call the overridden
methods.
•
• public class Main {
• public static void main(String[] args) {
• // Creating objects of Animal, Dog, and Cat
• Animal myAnimal = new Animal();
• Animal myDog = new Dog();
• Animal myCat = new Cat();
•• // Demonstrating polymorphism
• myAnimal.sound(); // Outputs: Animal makes a sound
• myDog.sound(); // Outputs: Dog barks
• myCat.sound(); // Outputs: Cat meows
•
• // Using the super keyword to call superclass method
• Dog specificDog = new Dog();
• specificDog.sound(); // Outputs: Dog barks
• // To call the superclass method, we would need to modify the Dog class
to include a call to super.sound()
• }
• }
•
• class Animal {
• // Method to be overridden
• public void sound() {
• System.out.println("Animal makes a sound");
• }
• }
•
• class Dog extends Animal {
• // Overriding the sound method
• @Override
• public void sound() {
• System.out.println("Dog barks");
• }
• }
•
• class Cat extends Animal {
• // Overriding the sound method
• @Override
• public void sound() {
• System.out.println("Cat meows");• }
• }
•
• // Note: The super keyword is used within a subclass to call a method from its
superclass.
• // In this example, we would modify the Dog class to demonstrate this, but for
simplicity, it is not shown here.
Hands-on: Overriding Methods in Java
• Create a superclass with a method to be overridden.
• Develop a subclass that overrides the superclass method.
• Utilize the super keyword to invoke the superclass method within the overridden method.
• Create subclass objects and call overridden methods through superclass references.
• Observe polymorphic behavior and runtime method determination.
• // This code demonstrates method overriding, the use of the super keyword, and
polymorphism in Java.
•
• // Main class with the main method
• public class MethodOverridingExample {
• public static void main(String[] args) {
• // Create an object of the subclass
• Dog myDog = new Dog();
•
• // Call the overridden method using the subclass object
• myDog.makeSound();
• // Output:
• // Animal makes a sound
• // Dog barks
•
• // Demonstrating polymorphism
• Animal myAnimal = new Dog(); // Superclass reference to a subclass
object
• myAnimal.makeSound();
• // Output:• // Animal makes a sound
• // Dog barks
• }
• }
•
• // Superclass definition
• class Animal {
• // Method to be overridden
• public void makeSound() {
• System.out.println("Animal makes a sound");
• }
• }
•
• // Subclass that overrides the makeSound method
• class Dog extends Animal {
• // Overriding the makeSound method
• @Override
• public void makeSound() {
• // Call the superclass method
• super.makeSound(); // Calls Animal's makeSound method
• // Add additional behavior
• System.out.println("Dog barks");
• }
• }
•
• // In this example, the Dog class overrides the makeSound method of the Animal
class.
• // The super keyword is used to call the original makeSound method from the
Animal class.
• // Polymorphism is demonstrated by using an Animal reference to call the
overridden method on a Dog object.
Introduction to Polymorphism• Polymorphism allows treating objects as instances of their parent class.
• A single interface can represent different underlying forms or data types.
• Enhances flexibility and maintainability in object-oriented programming.
• Enables writing generic and reusable code for scalable applications.
• Simplifies code management and extension, focusing on innovation.
Compile-time vs Runtime Polymorphism
• Compile-time polymorphism is achieved
through method overloading, where methods
have the same name but different parameters.
• Method overloading is resolved during
compilation based on method signatures.
• Runtime polymorphism is achieved through
method overriding, where a subclass provides
a specific implementation of a superclass
method.
• Method overriding is resolved during
execution, allowing for dynamic method
dispatch.
• Compile-time polymorphism ensures method resolution at compile time, while runtime
polymorphism allows for flexibility during execution.
Polymorphic Behavior in Java
• Polymorphic behavior enables dynamic method invocation at runtime.
• Objects can be referenced by their superclass type, allowing flexibility.
• Superclass references can point to subclass objects, invoking overridden methods.
• Facilitates writing generic and reusable code for different classes with a common superclass.
• Enhances code design by supporting uniform handling of related objects.
• Promotes maintainability and extensibility in software systems.
Dynamic Method Dispatch
• Dynamic method dispatch resolves overridden method calls at runtime, not compile-time.
• It is essential for runtime polymorphism in Java.
• Supports method overriding, allowing subclasses to provide specific implementations.
• JVM determines method execution based on actual object type, not reference type.
• Enhances flexibility and adaptability in Java's object-oriented programming.
• // This code demonstrates dynamic method dispatch in Java.
• // It shows how the JVM resolves method calls at runtime based on the actual
object type.
•
• public class DynamicMethodDispatchExample {
• public static void main(String[] args) {
• // Reference of type Animal pointing to an object of type Dog• Animal myAnimal = new Dog();
• // Calls the overridden method in Dog class
• myAnimal.sound(); // Output: Dog barks
•
• // Reference of type Animal pointing to an object of type Cat
• myAnimal = new Cat();
• // Calls the overridden method in Cat class
• myAnimal.sound(); // Output: Cat meows
•
• // Reference of type Animal pointing to an object of type Animal
• myAnimal = new Animal();
• // Calls the method in Animal class
• myAnimal.sound(); // Output: Animal makes a sound
• }
• }
•
• class Animal {
• // Method to be overridden
• void sound() {
• System.out.println("Animal makes a sound");
• }
• }
•
• class Dog extends Animal {
• // Overriding the sound method
• @Override
• void sound() {
• System.out.println("Dog barks");
• }
• }
•
• class Cat extends Animal {
• // Overriding the sound method• @Override
• void sound() {
• System.out.println("Cat meows");
• }
• }
•
• // In this example, the method `sound()` is overridden in both `Dog` and `Cat`
classes.
• // The `main` method demonstrates dynamic method dispatch by showing how the
JVM
• // determines which `sound()` method to call based on the actual object type at
runtime.
Benefits of Polymorphism
• Enhances code flexibility and reusability by treating objects as instances of their parent class.
• Allows for writing generic and reusable code across different classes.
• Simplifies code maintenance and readability by providing an abstract, high-level view.
• Facilitates elegant software designs, crucial for implementing design patterns.
• Supports modular and scalable software architecture, easing extension and maintenance.
Common Pitfalls in Polymorphism
• Misunderstanding reference vs object type can
lead to unexpected behavior.
• Overusing polymorphism may result in complex,
hard-to-maintain code.
• Static methods are not polymorphic; they are
resolved at compile-time.
• Reference type determines method execution
for static methods.
• Effective use of polymorphism requires
understanding its limitations.
Hands-on: Demonstrating Polymorphism
• Create a superclass and a subclass, each with an overridden method.
• Use a superclass reference to call a subclass method.
• Observe dynamic method dispatch in action, where the JVM determines the method to
execute at runtime.
• Understand how polymorphism enables flexible and reusable code in Java.
• Gain practical insights into the application of polymorphism in software design.// This code demonstrates polymorphism in Java using a superclass and a subclass.
// We will see how a superclass reference can be used to call a subclass method.
// Main class with the main method
public class PolymorphismExample {
public static void main(String[] args) {
// Create an instance of Dog using a superclass reference
Animal myAnimal = new Dog();
// Call the makeSound method
myAnimal.makeSound(); // Outputs: Dog barks
// Explanation:
// Even though the reference type is Animal, the actual object is of type Dog.
// Therefore, the Dog's version of makeSound() is called, demonstrating polymorphism.
}
}
// Define the superclass
class Animal {
// Method to be overridden
public void makeSound() {
System.out.println("Animal makes a sound");
}
}
// Define the subclass
class Dog extends Animal {
// Overriding the makeSound method
@Override
public void makeSound() {System.out.println("Dog barks");
}
}
Understanding Interfaces in Java
• Interfaces define a contract that classes must implement.
• Contain only abstract methods, promoting flexibility.
• Enable polymorphism by allowing different implementations.
• Support multiple inheritance, unlike classes.
• Crucial for achieving abstraction in Java.
Defining an Interface
• Define an interface using the 'interface' keyword.
• Interfaces contain abstract methods, implicitly public and abstract.
• Include constants in interfaces, implicitly public, static, and final.
• Interfaces cannot have constructors as they cannot be instantiated.
• // This code example demonstrates how to define and implement an interface in
Java.
•
• // Main class to demonstrate the use of the interface and its implementations
• public class Main {
• public static void main(String[] args) {
• // Create instances of Car and Motorcycle
• Vehicle myCar = new Car();
• Vehicle myMotorcycle = new Motorcycle();
•
• // Call the startEngine method on both instances
• myCar.startEngine(); // Output: Car engine started.
• myMotorcycle.startEngine(); // Output: Motorcycle engine started.
•
• // Access the constant from the interface
• System.out.println("Maximum speed: " + Vehicle.MAX_SPEED); // Output:
Maximum speed: 120
• }
• }
•
• // Define an interface named 'Vehicle'• interface Vehicle {
• // Abstract method (implicitly public and abstract)
• void startEngine();
•
• // Constant (implicitly public, static, and final)
• int MAX_SPEED = 120;
• }
•
• // Implement the 'Vehicle' interface in a class named 'Car'
• class Car implements Vehicle {
• // Provide a concrete implementation of the 'startEngine' method
• @Override
• public void startEngine() {
• System.out.println("Car engine started.");
• }
• }
•
• // Implement the 'Vehicle' interface in another class named 'Motorcycle'
• class Motorcycle implements Vehicle {
• // Provide a concrete implementation of the 'startEngine' method
• @Override
• public void startEngine() {
• System.out.println("Motorcycle engine started.");
• }
• }
Implementing Interfaces
• Use the 'implements' keyword to allow a class to inherit abstract methods from an interface.
• A class can implement multiple interfaces, enabling it to inherit diverse functionalities.
• Concrete implementations of all abstract methods in the interface are mandatory for the
implementing class.
• Interfaces facilitate polymorphism by allowing different classes to implement the same
interface in unique ways.
• // This code example demonstrates how to implement interfaces in Java.• // It shows a class implementing multiple interfaces and providing concrete
implementations for their methods.
•
• // Define a class that implements both interfaces
• public class Dog implements Animal, Movable {
• // Provide implementation for the eat method from Animal interface
• @Override
• public void eat() {
• System.out.println("The dog is eating."); // Output: The dog is eating.
• }
•
• // Provide implementation for the move method from Movable interface
• @Override
• public void move() {
• System.out.println("The dog is moving."); // Output: The dog is moving.
• }
•
• // Main method to execute the code
• public static void main(String[] args) {
• // Create an instance of Dog
• Dog myDog = new Dog();
•
• // Call the methods implemented from the interfaces
• myDog.eat(); // Calls the eat method
• myDog.move(); // Calls the move method
• }
• }
•
• // Define the first interface
• interface Animal {
• void eat(); // abstract method
• }
•• // Define the second interface
• interface Movable {
• void move(); // abstract method
• }
•
• // This code demonstrates polymorphism by allowing the Dog class to implement
multiple interfaces.
• // The Dog class provides specific implementations for the methods defined in
the Animal and Movable interfaces.
• // This allows for flexible and dynamic method invocation based on the
interface type.
Interface vs Abstract Class
• Interfaces contain only abstract methods; abstract classes can have both abstract and
concrete methods.
• A class can implement multiple interfaces but can extend only one abstract class.
• Interfaces cannot have constructors; abstract classes can have constructors.
• Use interfaces to define shared capabilities across classes.
• Use abstract classes for shared base functionality among related classes.
• // This code example demonstrates the use of interfaces and abstract classes in
Java.
•
• // Main class containing the main method
• public class Main {
• public static void main(String[] args) {
• // Create instances of Bird and Plane
• Bird bird = new Bird();
• Plane plane = new Plane();
•
• // Call the fly method on both instances
• bird.fly(); // Output: Bird is flying using wings.
• plane.fly(); // Output: Plane is flying using engines.
•
• // Create instances of Car and Bike
• Car car = new Car();• Bike bike = new Bike();
•
• // Call the start method on both instances
• car.start(); // Output: Car engine starting.
• bike.start(); // Output: Bike engine starting.
• }
• }
•
• // Interface defining a Flyable capability
• interface Flyable {
• void fly(); // Abstract method to be implemented by classes
• }
•
• // Class Bird implementing the Flyable interface
• class Bird implements Flyable {
• @Override
• public void fly() {
• System.out.println("Bird is flying using wings.");
• }
• }
•
• // Class Plane implementing the Flyable interface
• class Plane implements Flyable {
• @Override
• public void fly() {
• System.out.println("Plane is flying using engines.");
• }
• }
•
• // Abstract class Vehicle defining a common base for vehicles
• abstract class Vehicle {
• // Abstract method to be implemented by subclasses
• abstract void start();•
• // Concrete method that can be used by subclasses
• void stop() {
• System.out.println("Vehicle is stopping.");
• }
• }
•
• // Class Car extending the abstract class Vehicle
• class Car extends Vehicle {
• @Override
• void start() {
• System.out.println("Car engine starting.");
• }
• }
•
• // Class Bike extending the abstract class Vehicle
• class Bike extends Vehicle {
• @Override
• void start() {
• System.out.println("Bike engine starting.");
• }
• }
Default and Static Methods in Interfaces
• Java 8 introduced default and static methods to enhance interfaces.
• These features allow interfaces to evolve without breaking existing code.
• Default methods provide a default implementation that can be overridden.
• Static methods belong to the interface and can be called without an instance.
• Enhances interface functionality with utility methods and default behavior.
• // This code example demonstrates the use of default and static methods in Java
8 interfaces.
•
• // Main class containing the main method
• public class InterfaceEnhancementsExample {• public static void main(String[] args) {
• // Create an instance of the implementing class
• MyClass myClass = new MyClass();
•
• // Call the default method
• myClass.defaultMethod(); // Output: Default implementation in
MyInterface
•
• // Call the overridden method
• myClass.overriddenMethod(); // Output: Overridden implementation in
MyClass
•
• // Call the static method from the interface
• MyInterface.staticMethod(); // Output: Static method in MyInterface
• }
• }
•
• // Define an interface with a default method and a static method
• interface MyInterface {
• // Default method with a default implementation
• default void defaultMethod() {
• System.out.println("Default implementation in MyInterface");
• }
•
• // Abstract method that must be implemented by the implementing class
• void overriddenMethod();
•
• // Static method that belongs to the interface
• static void staticMethod() {
• System.out.println("Static method in MyInterface");
• }
• }
•• // Implementing class that implements MyInterface
• class MyClass implements MyInterface {
• // Override the abstract method
• @Override
• public void overriddenMethod() {
• System.out.println("Overridden implementation in MyClass");
• }
•
• // Optionally, override the default method
• // Uncomment the following method to see the overridden behavior
• // @Override
• // public void defaultMethod() {
• // System.out.println("Overridden default method in MyClass");
• // }
• }
Real-world Use Cases of Interfaces
• Interfaces are integral to the Java Collections Framework, allowing interchangeable use of
collection types.
• Enable multiple inheritance of type, promoting flexible and reusable code design.
• Facilitate loose coupling, enhancing modularity and testability of code.
• Commonly used in API and framework design for standardized component interaction.
• // This code example demonstrates the use of interfaces in Java to achieve
flexibility and reusability.
• // We will create a simple example using the Java Collections Framework to show
how interfaces allow
• // different collection types to be used interchangeably.
•
• import java.util.List;
• import java.util.ArrayList;
• import java.util.LinkedList;
•
• // Main class containing the main method
• public class InterfaceExample {• public static void main(String[] args) {
• // Using List interface to hold an ArrayList
• List<String> arrayList = new ArrayList<>();
• arrayList.add("Apple");
• arrayList.add("Banana");
• arrayList.add("Cherry");
•
• // Using List interface to hold a LinkedList
• List<String> linkedList = new LinkedList<>();
• linkedList.add("Dog");
• linkedList.add("Elephant");
• linkedList.add("Frog");
•
• // Displaying elements of both lists
• System.out.println("ArrayList elements:");
• printList(arrayList); // Output: Apple, Banana, Cherry
•
• System.out.println("LinkedList elements:");
• printList(linkedList); // Output: Dog, Elephant, Frog
• }
•
• // Method to print elements of a list
• public static void printList(List<String> list) {
• for (String element : list) {
• System.out.println(element);
• }
• }
• }

implementations
• // (ArrayList and LinkedList) interchangeably. This is a demonstration of the
flexibility• // and reusability that interfaces provide in Java, which is crucial for
designing modular
• // and maintainable code.
Hands-on: Creating and Using Interfaces
• Define an interface as a contract with abstract methods.
• Create a class that implements the interface.
• Test the class by creating objects and calling the implemented methods.
// Define an interface
interface Animal {
// Abstract method
void makeSound();
// Default method with implementation
default void sleep() {
System.out.println("Zzz");
}
}
// Implement the interface in a class
class Dog implements Animal {
// Provide implementation for the abstract method
public void makeSound() {
System.out.println("Woof");
}
}
public class Main {
public static void main(String[] args) {
// Create an instance of the Dog class
Dog dog = new Dog();
// Call the implemented methoddog.makeSound(); // Outputs: Woof
// Call the default method
dog.sleep(); // Outputs: Zzz
}
}
Concept of Multiple Interfaces
• Java allows a class to implement multiple interfaces, enabling behavior inheritance from
multiple sources.
• This approach facilitates a form of multiple inheritance, which is not directly supported
through classes in Java.
• Implementing multiple interfaces allows a class to adhere to multiple contracts, enhancing
versatility and flexibility.
• Crucial for scenarios where a class needs to exhibit behaviors from different interfaces,
promoting modular and reusable code.
• Enables clean separation of concerns, making code easier to maintain and extend.
Syntax for Implementing Multiple Interfaces
• Use the 'implements' keyword to declare interface implementation.
• List multiple interfaces separated by commas after 'implements'.
• Provide concrete implementations for all methods in the interfaces.
• Implementing interfaces allows a class to fulfill multiple contracts.
• Enhances class versatility and adherence to polymorphism principles.
• // This code example demonstrates how a class can implement multiple interfaces
in Java.
•
• // Main class implementing both interfaces
• public class Document implements Printable, Showable {
• // Implementing the print method from Printable interface
• public void print() {
• System.out.println("Printing the document..."); // Output for print
method
• }
•
• // Implementing the show method from Showable interface
• public void show() {
• System.out.println("Showing the document..."); // Output for show
method• }
•
• // Main method to execute the code
• public static void main(String[] args) {
• Document doc = new Document(); // Create an instance of Document
• doc.print(); // Call the print method
• doc.show(); // Call the show method
• }
• }
•
• // Define the first interface
• interface Printable {
• void print(); // Method to be implemented
• }
•
• // Define the second interface
• interface Showable {
• void show(); // Method to be implemented
• }
•
• // When executed, this code will output:
• // Printing the document...
• // Showing the document...
• // This demonstrates how a single class can implement multiple interfaces and
fulfill their contracts.
Resolving Method Conflicts
• Method conflicts arise when multiple interfaces have methods with identical signatures.
• Resolve conflicts by providing a single implementation in the class.
• Java 8 introduced default methods in interfaces to offer default implementations.
• Default methods can be overridden, enhancing flexibility and avoiding conflicts.
• Using default methods leads to more robust and maintainable code.
• // This code example demonstrates how a class can implement multiple interfaces• // with default methods and resolve method conflicts by providing its own
implementation.
•
• // Main class with the main method
• public class MethodConflictResolution {
• public static void main(String[] args) {
• // Create an instance of the implementing class
• MyClass myClass = new MyClass();
•
• // Call the method that resolves the conflict
• myClass.conflictingMethod(); // Output: "MyClass implementation"
• }
• }
•
• // First interface with a default method
• interface InterfaceA {
• default void conflictingMethod() {
• System.out.println("InterfaceA default implementation");
• }
• }
•
• // Second interface with a default method
• interface InterfaceB {
• default void conflictingMethod() {
• System.out.println("InterfaceB default implementation");
• }
• }
•
• // Class implementing both interfaces
• class MyClass implements InterfaceA, InterfaceB {
• // Provide a unified implementation to resolve the method conflict
• @Override
• public void conflictingMethod() {• System.out.println("MyClass implementation");
• }
• }
•
• /*
• * In this example, MyClass implements both InterfaceA and InterfaceB,
• * each of which has a default method with the same signature.
• * MyClass resolves the conflict by providing its own implementation
• * of the conflictingMethod, ensuring consistent behavior.
• */
Benefits of Multiple Interfaces
• Enhances code reusability and modularity by inheriting behaviors from multiple sources.
• Allows a class to perform various tasks defined by different interfaces, increasing versatility.
• Facilitates flexible and scalable design, enabling easy construction of complex systems.
• Supports modular development, simplifying maintenance and expansion of functionality.
Common Mistakes with Multiple Interfaces
• Ensure all interface methods are implemented to avoid compilation errors.
• Understand the difference between interface and class inheritance.
• Avoid confusing interface inheritance with class inheritance.
• Resolve method conflicts when implementing multiple interfaces.
• Be mindful of method conflicts to prevent unexpected behavior.
Introduction to Abstract Classes
• Abstract classes cannot be instantiated directly; they serve as a blueprint.
• They provide a foundation for other classes to build upon.
• Contain both abstract methods (without a body) and concrete methods (with
implementation).
• Define common characteristics and behaviors for subclasses.
• Promote organized, modular code structure, enhancing code reuse and reducing
redundancy.
• abstract class Shape {
• // Abstract method (does not have a body)
• abstract void draw();
•
• // Regular method
• void displayShape() {
• System.out.println("This is a shape");• }
• }
•
• class Circle extends Shape {
• // The body of the draw() method is provided here
• void draw() {
• System.out.println("Drawing a circle");
• }
• }
•
• class Main {
• public static void main(String[] args) {
• Circle circle = new Circle();
• circle.draw(); // Output: Drawing a circle
• circle.displayShape(); // Output: This is a shape
• }
• }
Defining Abstract Methods
• Abstract methods are declared without a body and end with a semicolon.
• Subclasses must implement abstract methods, ensuring specific behaviors.
• Enforces a contract for subclasses, maintaining uniformity and flexibility.
• Allows for consistent behavior across subclasses with varied implementations.
• Useful in large systems for structured and reliable codebases.
• // This code example demonstrates the use of abstract methods in Java.
• // We define an abstract class 'Animal' with an abstract method 'makeSound'.
• // Subclasses 'Dog' and 'Cat' provide their own implementations of 'makeSound'.
• public class Main {
• public static void main(String[] args) {
• // Create a Dog object
• Animal myDog = new Dog();
• myDog.makeSound(); // Outputs "Woof"
• myDog.sleep(); // Outputs "Zzz"
•• // Create a Cat object
• Animal myCat = new Cat();
• myCat.makeSound(); // Outputs "Meow"
• myCat.sleep(); // Outputs "Zzz"
• }
• }
• abstract class Animal {
• // Abstract method (does not have a body)
• abstract void makeSound();
•
• // Regular method
• void sleep() {
• System.out.println("Zzz");
• }
• }
•
• // Subclass (inherited from Animal)
• class Dog extends Animal {
• // The body of makeSound() is provided here
• void makeSound() {
• System.out.println("Woof"); // Dog's implementation of makeSound
• }
• }
•
• // Another subclass (inherited from Animal)
• class Cat extends Animal {
• // The body of makeSound() is provided here
• void makeSound() {
• System.out.println("Meow"); // Cat's implementation of makeSound
• }
• }
Abstract Class vs Interface• Abstract classes can contain both abstract and concrete methods, allowing shared code
among subclasses.
• Interfaces traditionally had only abstract methods but can now include default and static
methods (Java 8+).
• A class can extend only one abstract class due to single inheritance.
• A class can implement multiple interfaces, allowing for more flexible design.
• Interfaces provide a way to define a contract for classes without dictating implementation.
• // This code example demonstrates the use of abstract classes and interfaces in
Java.
• // It highlights the differences and use cases for each.
•
• // Main class containing the main method
• public class Main {
• public static void main(String[] args) {
• // Create an instance of a concrete class that extends an abstract
class
• Dog dog = new Dog();
• dog.makeSound(); // Calls the concrete method from the abstract class
• dog.eat(); // Calls the abstract method implemented in the Dog class
•
• // Create an instance of a class that implements multiple interfaces
• Robot robot = new Robot();
• robot.start(); // Calls the method from the Machine interface
• robot.stop(); // Calls the method from the Machine interface
• robot.charge(); // Calls the method from the Chargeable interface
• }
• }
•
• // Abstract class with both abstract and concrete methods
• abstract class Animal {
• // Abstract method (no implementation)
• abstract void makeSound();
•
• // Concrete method
• void eat() {• System.out.println("This animal is eating.");
• }
• }
•
• // Concrete class that extends the abstract class
• class Dog extends Animal {
• // Implementing the abstract method
• @Override
• void makeSound() {
• System.out.println("Woof Woof");
• }
• }
•
• // Interface with abstract methods
• interface Machine {
• void start();
• void stop();
• }
•
• // Interface with a default method
• interface Chargeable {
• void charge();
•
• // Default method
• default void plugIn() {
• System.out.println("Plugging in the charger.");
• }
• }
•
• // Class implementing multiple interfaces
• class Robot implements Machine, Chargeable {
• // Implementing methods from the Machine interface
• @Override• public void start() {
• System.out.println("Robot starting.");
• }
•
• @Override
• public void stop() {
• System.out.println("Robot stopping.");
• }
•
• // Implementing method from the Chargeable interface
• @Override
• public void charge() {
• System.out.println("Robot charging.");
• }
• }
When to Use Abstract Classes
• Abstract classes enable code sharing among closely related classes.
• They serve as a common base class with shared methods and fields.
• Promote code reuse and consistency across subclasses.
• Allow definition of non-static or non-final fields, unlike interfaces.
• Use abstract classes when you have closely related classes sharing common
code.
• Abstract classes are ideal when you want to provide a common interface as
well as some common functionalities.
• They are useful when you need to define non-static fields that can be shared
and modified by subclasses.
• Abstract classes help maintain consistency across subclasses.
abstract class Vehicle {
// A non-static field that's shared across subclasses
protected int speed;
// Abstract method (does not have a body)
abstract void accelerate();// Concrete method with common implementation
void stop() {
speed = 0;
System.out.println("Vehicle stopped.");
}
}
class Car extends Vehicle {
@Override
void accelerate() {
speed += 20;
System.out.println("Car accelerates to " + speed + " km/h.");
}
}
class Bicycle extends Vehicle {
@Override
void accelerate() {
speed += 5;
System.out.println("Bicycle accelerates to " + speed + " km/h.");
}
}
class Main {
public static void main(String[] args) {
Car car = new Car();
car.accelerate(); // Output: Car accelerates to 20 km/h.
car.stop(); // Output: Vehicle stopped.
Bicycle bicycle = new Bicycle();
bicycle.accelerate(); // Output: Bicycle accelerates to 5 km/h.
bicycle.stop();}
}
Benefits and Limitations of Abstract Classes
• Abstract classes promote code reuse by sharing methods across subclasses.
• They provide an organized structure, serving as a blueprint for subclasses.
• Enforce method implementation, ensuring subclasses implement necessary methods.
• Support single inheritance, limiting a class to extend only one abstract class.
• Cannot be instantiated directly, which may limit flexibility in certain scenarios.
Hands-on: Creating Abstract Classes
• Create an abstract class 'Shape' with an abstract method 'draw()' to serve as a blueprint.
• Implement concrete subclasses like 'Circle' and 'Rectangle' that extend 'Shape'.
• Override the 'draw()' method in each subclass to provide specific implementations.
• Understand how abstract classes enforce a consistent interface while allowing unique
subclass implementations.
• // This Java program demonstrates the use of abstract classes and methods.
• // We define an abstract class 'Shape' with an abstract method 'draw()'.
• // Then, we create concrete subclasses 'Circle' and 'Rectangle' that implement
the 'draw()' method.
•
• // Main class containing the main method
• public class Main {
• public static void main(String[] args) {
• // Create instances of Circle and Rectangle
• Shape circle = new Circle();
• Shape rectangle = new Rectangle();
•
• // Call the draw method on each instance
• circle.draw(); // Output: Drawing a Circle
• rectangle.draw(); // Output: Drawing a Rectangle
• }
• }
•
• // Abstract class Shape
• abstract class Shape {
• // Abstract method draw• abstract void draw();
• }
•
• // Concrete subclass Circle extending Shape
• class Circle extends Shape {
• // Implementing the abstract method draw
• @Override
• void draw() {
• System.out.println("Drawing a Circle");
• }
• }
•
• // Concrete subclass Rectangle extending Shape
• class Rectangle extends Shape {
• // Implementing the abstract method draw
• @Override
• void draw() {
• System.out.println("Drawing a Rectangle");
• }
• }
Understanding Composition in Java
• Composition involves creating a class using objects from other classes.
• Represents a 'has-a' relationship, unlike inheritance's 'is-a' relationship.
• Enhances flexibility and reusability in code design.
• Allows for creating complex types by combining different objects.
• Promotes the use of interfaces and delegation for maintainable code.
• // This code example demonstrates the concept of composition in Java.
• // We will create a Car class that is composed of Engine, Wheel, and Seat
objects.
•
• // Main class containing the main method
• public class CarCompositionExample {
• public static void main(String[] args) {• // Create components of the car
• Engine engine = new Engine("V8", 500);
• Wheel wheel = new Wheel(18);
• Seat seat = new Seat("Leather");
•
• // Create a car using composition
• Car car = new Car(engine, wheel, seat);
•
• // Display car details
• car.displayCarDetails(); // Output the details of the car
• }
• }
•

Composition vs Inheritance
• Inheritance derives a new class from an existing one, creating an 'is-a' relationship.
• Composition builds classes using objects from other classes, forming a 'has-a' relationship.
• Inheritance can lead to tight coupling and less flexible code.
• Composition is preferred for flexibility, allowing modular and adaptable designs.
• Changing behavior in composition is easier by altering composed objects, not the class itself.
When to Use Composition
• Use composition to model a 'has-a' relationship, enhancing modularity.
• Prefer composition for its flexibility and reusability over rigid inheritance structures.
• Avoid deep inheritance hierarchies with composition for easier management.
• Ideal for runtime behavior changes by swapping components without altering class
structure.
Advantages of Composition
• Composition promotes code reuse and modularity, akin to assembling a puzzle with reusable
pieces.
• Enhances flexibility and adaptability by allowing components to be swapped or combined
easily.
• Reduces tight coupling between classes, leading to a more manageable and understandable
codebase.• Facilitates easier testing and maintenance by enabling isolated testing of individual
components.
• Enables the creation of robust, maintainable, and scalable software systems.
Design Patterns Using Composition
• Design patterns like Strategy and Decorator leverage composition for flexibility.
• Strategy pattern changes class behavior at runtime using composition.
• Decorator pattern adds functionality dynamically without altering structure.
• Composition enables flexible, reusable, and extendable design patterns.
• Key skill for Java developers: using composition in design patterns.
Hands-on: Implementing Composition
• Design a class that incorporates objects from other classes using composition.
• Implement methods that delegate tasks to these composed objects.
• Test the design's flexibility by swapping components to alter behavior.
• Explore the adaptability and reusability of composition-based designs.
• Understand how composition enhances software flexibility and maintainability.

Exception Handling and Debugging
What are Exceptions in Java?
• Exceptions disrupt the normal flow of a program, similar to roadblocks in driving.
• They are represented as objects that encapsulate error information.
• Java's robust mechanism handles exceptions, ensuring reliable code.
• Understanding exceptions is key to building error-resistant applications.
• // Importing necessary classes for exception handling
• import java.io.File;
• import java.io.FileNotFoundException;
• import java.util.Scanner;
•
• public class ExceptionHandlingExample {• public static void main(String[] args) {
• // Demonstrating a try-catch block to handle exceptions
• try {
• // Attempting to open a file that may not exist
• File file = new File("nonexistentfile.txt");
• Scanner scanner = new Scanner(file);
•
• // Reading from the file
• while (scanner.hasNextLine()) {
• String line = scanner.nextLine();
• System.out.println(line);
• }
• scanner.close();
• } catch (FileNotFoundException e) {
• // Handling the exception if the file is not found
• System.out.println("An error occurred: File not found.");
• }
•
• // Demonstrating a try-catch-finally block
• Scanner inputScanner = null;
• try {
• // Attempting to open a file that may not exist
• File file = new File("anotherfile.txt");
• inputScanner = new Scanner(file);
•
• // Reading from the file
• while (inputScanner.hasNextLine()) {
• String line = inputScanner.nextLine();
• System.out.println(line);
• }
• } catch (FileNotFoundException e) {
• // Handling the exception if the file is not found
• System.out.println("An error occurred: File not found.");• } finally {
• // Ensuring the scanner is closed to free resources
• if (inputScanner != null) {
• inputScanner.close();
• }
• }
• }
• }
Importance of Exception Handling
• Exception handling ensures application stability by managing unexpected errors.
• Allows programs to recover gracefully, enhancing user experience.
• Improves code readability by clearly defining error management strategies.
• Facilitates easier code maintenance and collaboration among developers.
• Essential for writing robust, maintainable, and user-friendly Java applications.
Checked vs Unchecked Exceptions
• Checked exceptions are verified by the compiler at compile-time.
• Must handle checked exceptions with try-catch or declare with 'throws'.
• Examples of checked exceptions: IOException, SQLException.
• Unchecked exceptions occur at runtime, not checked by the compiler.
• Result from programming errors like logic mistakes.
• Examples of unchecked exceptions: NullPointerException, ArithmeticException.
• Understanding these distinctions aids in robust error handling.
Common Java Exceptions
• NullPointerException occurs when accessing a method or field on a null object.
• ArrayIndexOutOfBoundsException is thrown for invalid array index access.
• IOException relates to failures in input/output operations, like file reading errors.
• Understanding these exceptions helps in anticipating and handling errors effectively.
Exception Hierarchy in Java
• Throwable is the root class for all exceptions and errors in Java.
• Error and Exception are the two main subclasses of Throwable.
• Errors are serious issues that applications should not handle.
• Exception is divided into checked and unchecked exceptions.
• Checked exceptions must be handled at compile time.
• Unchecked exceptions occur at runtime and are not checked at compile time.
How Java Handles Exceptions
• Java uses try-catch blocks to manage exceptions effectively.
• The try block contains code that might throw an exception, acting as a safety net.• If an exception occurs, the catch block handles it, preventing program crashes.
• Separating error-handling code from main logic enhances code organization and
maintainability.
• Try-catch blocks allow programs to continue executing after errors, improving stability.

• import java.util.Scanner;
•
• public class ExceptionHandlingExample {
• public static void main(String[] args) {

•
• // Prompting the user to enter a number

• int number = scanner.nextInt();
•
• // Performing division operation
• int result = 100 / number;
•
• // Printing the result of the division
• System.out.println("Result: " + result);
• } catch (ArithmeticException e) {
• // Handling division by zero exception
• System.out.println("Error: Cannot divide by zero.");
• } catch (Exception e) {
• // Handling any other exceptions
• System.out.println("Error: An unexpected error occurred.");

• }

Significance of Exception Handling in Applications
• Prevents unexpected application crashes and safeguards against data loss.
• Provides meaningful error messages to guide users and enhance their experience.
• Facilitates easier debugging and maintenance by clearly defining error management.
• Ensures applications remain robust and reliable through organized error handling.
Common Mistakes in Understanding Exceptions
• Ignoring exceptions can lead to application crashes and data loss.
• Broad catch blocks obscure the root cause of errors, complicating debugging.
• Not logging exceptions hinders future troubleshooting efforts.
• Handle exceptions with specific catch blocks for clarity.
• Log errors to provide insights for future issue resolution.
Syntax of try-catch Blocks
• try-catch blocks are used to handle exceptions in Java, ensuring program stability.
• Syntax involves a try block for code that might throw exceptions and catch blocks for
handling them.
• The try block contains code that could potentially cause an error.
• If an exception occurs, control moves to the catch block to handle it.
• This mechanism maintains the normal flow of the application, even when errors occur.
• // Importing necessary classes for input handling
• import java.util.Scanner;

How try-catch Blocks Work
• The try block contains code that might throw an exception.
• If an exception occurs, control shifts to the catch block.
• The catch block handles the exception, preventing abrupt termination.
• Catch blocks can log errors or provide user-friendly messages.
• If no exception occurs, the catch block is skipped.
• // Demonstrating the use of try-catch blocks in Java
• public class TryCatchExample {
• public static void main(String[] args) {
• // Segment 1: Try block to handle potential exceptions
• try {
• // Attempting to divide by zero, which will throw an exception• int result = 10 / 0;
• System.out.println("Result: " + result); // This line will not
execute
• } catch (ArithmeticException e) {
• // Segment 2: Catch block to handle the exception
• System.out.println("An error occurred: " + e.getMessage()); // Log
the error message
• }
• // Segment 3: Code after try-catch block
• System.out.println("Program continues smoothly after handling the
exception."); // Program continues
• }
• }
Catching Multiple Exceptions
• Multiple catch blocks can address different exception types.
• Each catch block is specific to a particular exception.
• Java 7 introduced multi-catch blocks for handling multiple exceptions together.
• Use'' to separate exception types in a multi-catch block.
• Multi-catch blocks reduce code duplication and improve readability.
• // Java program to demonstrate handling multiple exceptions using multi-catch
• public class MultiCatchExample {
• public static void main(String[] args) {
• // Array of integers
• int[] numbers = {10, 0, 5};
• // Index to access
• int index = 3;
•
• // Try block to handle exceptions
• try {
• // Attempt to divide by zero
• int result = numbers[0] / numbers[1];
• System.out.println("Result of division: " + result); // This line
may throw ArithmeticException•
• // Attempt to access an invalid index
• System.out.println("Accessing index: " + numbers[index]); // This
line may throw ArrayIndexOutOfBoundsException
• }
• // Multi-catch block to handle multiple exceptions
• catch (ArithmeticException | ArrayIndexOutOfBoundsException e) {
• // Print the exception message
• System.out.println("Exception caught: " + e.getMessage()); //
Execute this line to see the exception message
• }
•
• // Code after try-catch block
• System.out.println("Program continues after handling exceptions."); //
Execute this line to see the program continuation
• }
• }
Nested try-catch Blocks
• Nested try-catch blocks manage exceptions at different code levels.
• Inner try-catch handles specific exceptions for particular operations.
• Outer try-catch manages exceptions not caught by inner blocks.
• Ideal for complex operations with multiple potential exception types.
• Enhances control and precision in exception handling.
• // Import necessary classes for file handling
• import java.io.*;
•
• public class NestedTryCatchExample {
• public static void main(String[] args) {
• // Outer try-catch block to handle general exceptions
• try {
• // Inner try-catch block to handle file reading exceptions
• try {
• // Attempt to read from a file• BufferedReader reader = new BufferedReader(new
FileReader("input.txt"));
• String line = reader.readLine();
• System.out.println("Read from file: " + line);
• reader.close();
• } catch (FileNotFoundException e) {
• // Handle the case where the file is not found
• System.out.println("File not found: " + e.getMessage());
• }
•
• // Inner try-catch block to handle file writing exceptions
• try {
• // Attempt to write to a file
• BufferedWriter writer = new BufferedWriter(new
FileWriter("output.txt"));
• writer.write("Processed data");
• writer.close();
• System.out.println("Data written to file successfully.");
• } catch (IOException e) {
• // Handle I/O exceptions during writing
• System.out.println("Error writing to file: " + e.getMessage());
• }
• } catch (Exception e) {
• // Handle any other exceptions that were not caught by inner blocks
• System.out.println("An unexpected error occurred: " +
e.getMessage());
• }
• }
• }
Best Practices for try-catch Usage
• Use specific exception types in catch blocks for precise error handling.
• Avoid the generic Exception class to prevent hiding unexpected errors.• Keep try blocks small and focused for easier debugging.
• Log exceptions to aid in debugging and future analysis.
• // This Java program demonstrates best practices for using try-catch blocks
effectively.
• // It includes specific exception handling, small and focused try blocks, and
exception logging.
•
• import java.util.logging.Level;
• import java.util.logging.Logger;
•
• public class TryCatchBestPractices {
• private static final Logger logger =
Logger.getLogger(TryCatchBestPractices.class.getName());
•
• public static void main(String[] args) {
• // Example 1: Specific exception handling
• try {
• int[] numbers = {1, 2, 3};
• // This will throw ArrayIndexOutOfBoundsException
• System.out.println(numbers[3]);
• } catch (ArrayIndexOutOfBoundsException e) {
• logger.log(Level.SEVERE, "Array index is out of bounds: " +
e.getMessage());
• }
•
• // Example 2: Small and focused try block
• String str = null;
• try {
• // This will throw NullPointerException
• System.out.println(str.length());
• } catch (NullPointerException e) {
• logger.log(Level.SEVERE, "Attempted to access length of a null
string: " + e.getMessage());• }
•
• // Example 3: Logging exceptions
• try {
• int result = divide(10, 0); // This will throw ArithmeticException
• } catch (ArithmeticException e) {
• logger.log(Level.SEVERE, "Arithmetic error occurred: " +
e.getMessage());
• }
• }
•
• // A method to demonstrate exception handling in a separate method
• public static int divide(int a, int b) {
• return a / b; // Potential ArithmeticException if b is 0
• }
• }
•

keeping try blocks small and focused,
• // and logging exceptions to provide insights into errors. The logger outputs
the error messages to the console.

Common Mistakes with try-catch Blocks• Avoid catching the generic Exception class unnecessarily to prevent obscuring error causes.
• Swallowing exceptions without handling them can lead to silent failures and lack of feedback.
• Overusing try-catch blocks for control flow can complicate code readability and maintenance.
• Not logging exceptions hinders debugging and future analysis by leaving no error trace.
• // Demonstrating common mistakes with try-catch blocks in Java

• // Example 1: Catching a generic Exception
• try {

Output the error message

• int result = divide(10, 0); // This will cause an

Using Multiple catch Blocks
• Multiple catch blocks allow handling different exceptions separately.
• Each catch block is tailored to a specific exception type.
• Enhances code readability and precision in error handling.
• Ensures appropriate handling for each exception type.
• Improves application robustness by addressing specific scenarios.
• // Import necessary classes for file handling and SQL operations
• import java.io.File;
• import java.io.FileNotFoundException;
• import java.sql.Connection;
• import java.sql.DriverManager;
• import java.sql.SQLException;
•• public class ExceptionHandlingExample {
• public static void main(String[] args) {

• try {
• // Trying to open a file that may not exist

• System.out.println("Database connection successful.");
• } catch (FileNotFoundException e) {
• // Handle file not found exception

• System.out.println("Please check the file path and try again.");
• } catch (SQLException e) {
• // Handle SQL exception
• System.out.println("Error: " + e.getMessage());
• System.out.println("Database connection failed. Retrying...");
• // Retry logic or alternative handling can be implemented here
• } finally {
• // This block will always execute
• System.out.println("Execution completed. Cleaning up resources.");
• }
• }
• }Order of catch Blocks
• Catch blocks are evaluated sequentially in the order they appear.
• Specific exceptions should be caught before general ones to avoid errors.
• Catching a general exception first can lead to unreachable code errors.
• Proper ordering ensures the most appropriate catch block handles the exception.
• This approach maintains the integrity and efficiency of error handling.
• // Import necessary classes for exception handling
• import java.io.FileNotFoundException;
• import java.io.IOException;
•
• public class ExceptionHandlingExample {
• public static void main(String[] args) {
• // Try block to handle exceptions
• try {
• // Simulate a method that may throw exceptions
• simulateException();
• }
• // Catch block for specific exception
• catch (FileNotFoundException e) {
• // Handle FileNotFoundException
• System.out.println("File not found: " + e.getMessage());
• }
• // Catch block for more general exception
• catch (IOException e) {
• // Handle IOException
• System.out.println("Input/output error: " + e.getMessage());
• }
• // Catch block for the most general exception
• catch (Exception e) {
• // Handle any other exceptions
• System.out.println("An error occurred: " + e.getMessage());
• }
• }

• public static void simulateException() throws FileNotFoundException,
IOException {
• // Simulate a condition that throws FileNotFoundException
• throw new FileNotFoundException("Sample.txt not found");
• }
• }

The finally Block and Its Purpose
• Finally block executes after try-catch, regardless of exceptions.
• Performs cleanup activities like closing files or releasing resources.
• Ensures program maintains a consistent state.
• Guarantees code execution for resource management.
• Prevents resource leaks and maintains application stability.
• // Import necessary classes for file handling
• import java.io.File;
• import java.io.FileNotFoundException;
• import java.util.Scanner;
•
• public class FinallyBlockExample {
• public static void main(String[] args) {
• // Declare a Scanner object for reading a file
• Scanner fileReader = null;
• try {
• // Attempt to open a file
• File file = new File("example.txt");
• fileReader = new Scanner(file);
• // Read and print the first line of the file
• System.out.println(fileReader.nextLine());
• } catch (FileNotFoundException e) {
• // Handle the case where the file is not found
• System.out.println("File not found: " + e.getMessage());
• } finally {• // Ensure the Scanner is closed to release the resource
• if (fileReader != null) {
• fileReader.close();
• System.out.println("Scanner closed.");
• }
• }
• }
• }
When to Use finally
• Finally block ensures resource cleanup, like closing files or database connections.
• Prevents resource leaks by guaranteeing resource deallocation, even if exceptions occur.
• Crucial for maintaining application stability and performance.
• Acts as a safety net for executing critical cleanup code.
• Essential for building robust and reliable applications.
• // Import necessary classes for file handling
• import java.io.File;
• import java.io.FileNotFoundException;
• import java.util.Scanner;
•
• public class FinallyBlockExample {
• public static void main(String[] args) {
• // Initialize a Scanner object to null
• Scanner scanner = null;
• try {
• // Attempt to open a file and read its contents
• File file = new File("example.txt");
• scanner = new Scanner(file);
• while (scanner.hasNextLine()) {
• String line = scanner.nextLine();
• System.out.println(line);
• }
• } catch (FileNotFoundException e) {
• // Handle the case where the file is not found• System.out.println("File not found: " + e.getMessage());
• } finally {
• // Ensure the scanner is closed to free up resources
• if (scanner != null) {
• scanner.close();
• System.out.println("Scanner closed.");
• }
• }
• }
• }
Hands-on: Implementing finally Block
• Practice using the finally block for effective resource management.
• Implement a try-catch-finally structure in a sample program, such as file handling.
• Observe execution flow with and without exceptions to see the finally block's role.
• Ensure cleanup code runs in both scenarios, maintaining application stability.
• Understand the critical role of the finally block in Java exception handling.
• // Import necessary classes for file handling
• import java.io.File;
• import java.io.FileNotFoundException;
• import java.util.Scanner;
•
• public class FileHandlingExample {
• public static void main(String[] args) {
• // Declare the Scanner object outside the try block for wider scope
• Scanner scanner = null;
• try {
• // Attempt to open a file
• File file = new File("example.txt");
• scanner = new Scanner(file);
• // Read and print the file content
• while (scanner.hasNextLine()) {
• String line = scanner.nextLine();
• System.out.println(line);• }
• } catch (FileNotFoundException e) {
• // Handle the exception if the file is not found
• System.out.println("An error occurred: File not found.");
• } finally {
• // Ensure the scanner is closed to release the resource
• if (scanner != null) {
• scanner.close();
• System.out.println("Scanner closed.");
• }
• }
• // Execute the code to see the output
• }
• }
Common Mistakes with Multiple catch and finally
• Avoid catching general exceptions before specific ones to prevent unreachable code errors.
• Ensure resources are closed in the finally block to prevent resource leaks.
• Recognize that the finally block may not execute if the program exits abruptly, such as with
System.exit().
• Being aware of these pitfalls helps in writing robust and error-free Java code.
Throwing Exceptions in Java
• Exceptions disrupt the normal flow of a program and signal error conditions.
• Java allows explicit throwing of exceptions using the throw keyword.
• Throwing exceptions helps handle errors gracefully and maintain program control.
• Custom exceptions can be created for specific error scenarios, enhancing code robustness.

Using the throw Keyword
• The throw keyword explicitly throws an exception in Java.
• It can be used for both checked and unchecked exceptions.
• Syntax: Create a new exception instance with an error message.
• Example: throw new IllegalArgumentException("Invalid input provided");
• Useful in methods to indicate error conditions and allow handling.
• Enables controlled and predictable error response in programs.
• // Import necessary classes for handling exceptions• import java.io.IOException;

The throws Clause in Method Signatures
• The throws clause is part of method signatures to declare potential exceptions.• Indicates that a method might throw certain exceptions, especially checked ones.
• Checked exceptions must be declared using the throws clause.
• Syntax: return Type methodName() throws ExceptionType.
• Informs method callers about potential exceptions for proper handling.

Checked Exceptions and throws
• Checked exceptions are verified at compile-time by the compiler.
• Must be caught in a try-catch block or declared with throws.
• Promotes robust error handling and reliable applications.
• Examples include IOException and SQLException.
• Ensures developers address potential error conditions proactively.
• // Import necessary classes for handling exceptions
• import java.io.BufferedReader;
• import java.io.FileReader;
• import java.io.IOException;
•
• // Define a class to demonstrate checked exceptions

• // Method to read a file and handle IOException
• public static void readFile(String filePath) throws IOException {
